crsnk10{_}1526{_}The Board of Health carries on agriculture, stock-raising, and dairying, for local use, and employment at fair wages is furnished to all that wish to work.
crsnk10{_}1527{_}They are not compelled to work, however, for they are the wards of the territory.
crsnk10{_}1528{_}For the young, and the very old, and the helpless there are homes and hospitals.
crsnk10{_}1529{_}Major Lee, an American and long a marine engineer for the Inter Island Steamship Company, I met actively at work in the new steam laundry, where he was busy installing the machinery.
crsnk10{_}1530{_}I met him often, afterwards, and one day he said to me:  "Give us a good breeze about how we live here.
crsnk10{_}1531{_}For heaven's sake write us up straight.
crsnk10{_}1532{_}Put your foot down on this chamber-of-horrors rot and all the rest of it.
crsnk10{_}1533{_}We don't like being misrepresented.
crsnk10{_}1534{_}We've got some feelings.
crsnk10{_}1535{_}Just tell the world how we really are in here."
crsnk10{_}1536{_}Man after man that I met in the Settlement, and woman after woman, in one way or another expressed the same sentiment.
crsnk10{_}1537{_}It was patent that they resented bitterly the sensational and untruthful way in which they have been exploited in the past.
crsnk10{_}1538{_}In spite of the fact that they are afflicted by disease, the lepers form a happy colony, divided into two villages and numerous country and seaside homes, of nearly a thousand souls.
crsnk10{_}1539{_}They have six churches, a Young Men's Christian Association building, several assembly halls, a band stand, a race-track, baseball grounds, shooting ranges, an athletic club, numerous glee clubs, and two brass bands.
crsnk10{_}1540{_}"They are so contented down there," Mr. Pinkham told me, "that you can't drive them away with a shot-gun."
crsnk10{_}1541{_}This I later verified for myself.
crsnk10{_}1542{_}In January of this year, eleven of the lepers, on whom the disease, after having committed certain ravages, showed no further signs of activity, were brought back to Honolulu for re-examination.
crsnk10{_}1543{_}They were loath to come; and, on being asked whether or not they wanted to go free if found clean of leprosy, one and all answered, "Back to Molokai."
crsnk10{_}1544{_}In the old days, before the discovery of the leprosy bacillus, a small number of men and women, suffering from various and wholly different diseases, were adjudged lepers and sent to Molokai.
crsnk10{_}1545{_}Years afterward they suffered great consternation when the bacteriologists declared that they were not afflicted with leprosy and never had been.
crsnk10{_}1546{_}They fought against being sent away from Molokai, and in one way or another, as helpers and nurses, they got jobs from the Board of Health and remained.
crsnk10{_}1547{_}The present jailer is one of these men.
crsnk10{_}1548{_}Declared to be a non-leper, he accepted, on salary, the charge of the jail, in order to escape being sent away.
crsnk10{_}1549{_}At the present moment, in Honolulu, there is a bootblack.
crsnk10{_}1550{_}He is an American negro.
crsnk10{_}1551{_}Mr. McVeigh told me about him.
crsnk10{_}1552{_}Long ago, before the bacteriological tests, he was sent to Molokai as a leper.
crsnk10{_}1553{_}As a ward of the state he developed a superlative degree of independence and fomented much petty mischief.
crsnk10{_}1554{_}And then, one day, after having been for years a perennial source of minor annoyances, the bacteriological test was applied, and he was declared a non-leper.
crsnk10{_}1555{_}"Ah, ha!"
crsnk10{_}1556{_}chortled Mr. McVeigh.
crsnk10{_}1557{_}"Now I've got you!
crsnk10{_}1558{_}Out you go on the next steamer and good riddance!"
crsnk10{_}1559{_}But the negro didn't want to go.
crsnk10{_}1560{_}Immediately he married an old woman, in the last stages of leprosy, and began petitioning the Board of Health for permission to remain and nurse his sick wife.
crsnk10{_}1561{_}There was no one, he said pathetically, who could take care of his poor wife as well as he could.
crsnk10{_}1562{_}But they saw through his game, and he was deported on the steamer and given the freedom of the world.
crsnk10{_}1563{_}But he preferred Molokai.
crsnk10{_}1564{_}Landing on the leeward side of Molokai, he sneaked down the pali one night and took up his abode in the Settlement.
crsnk10{_}1565{_}He was apprehended, tried and convicted of trespass, sentenced to pay a small fine, and again deported on the steamer with the warning that if he trespassed again, he would be fined one hundred dollars and be sent to prison in Honolulu.
crsnk10{_}1566{_}And now, when Mr. McVeigh comes up to Honolulu, the bootblack shines his shoes for him and says:  "Say, Boss, I lost a good home down there.
crsnk10{_}1567{_}Yes, sir, I lost a good home."
crsnk10{_}1568{_}Then his voice sinks to a confidential whisper as he says, "Say, Boss, can't I go back?
crsnk10{_}1569{_}Can't you fix it for me so as I can go back?"
crsnk10{_}1570{_}He had lived nine years on Molokai, and he had had a better time there than he has ever had, before and after, on the outside.
crsnk10{_}1571{_}As regards the fear of leprosy itself, nowhere in the Settlement among lepers, or non-lepers, did I see any sign of it.
crsnk10{_}1572{_}The chief horror of leprosy obtains in the minds of those who have never seen a leper and who do not know anything about the disease.
crsnk10{_}1573{_}At the hotel at Waikiki a lady expressed shuddering amazement at my having the hardihood to pay a visit to the Settlement.
crsnk10{_}1574{_}On talking with her I learned that she had been born in Honolulu, had lived there all her life, and had never laid eyes on a leper.
crsnk10{_}1575{_}That was more than I could say of myself in the United States, where the segregation of lepers is loosely enforced and where I have repeatedly seen lepers on the streets of large cities.
crsnk10{_}1576{_}Leprosy is terrible, there is no getting away from that; but from what little I know of the disease and its degree of contagiousness, I would by far prefer to spend the rest of my days in Molokai than in any tuberculosis sanatorium.
crsnk10{_}1577{_}In every city and county hospital for poor people in the United States, or in similar institutions in other countries, sights as terrible as those in Molokai can be witnessed, and the sum total of these sights is vastly more terrible.
crsnk10{_}1578{_}For that matter, if it were given me to choose between being compelled to live in Molokai for the rest of my life, or in the East End of London, the East Side of New York, or the Stockyards of Chicago, I would select Molokai without debate.
crsnk10{_}1579{_}I would prefer one year of life in Molokai to five years of life in the above- mentioned cesspools of human degradation and misery.
crsnk10{_}1580{_}In Molokai the people are happy.
crsnk10{_}1581{_}I shall never forget the celebration of the Fourth of July I witnessed there.
crsnk10{_}1582{_}At six o'clock in the morning the "horribles" were out, dressed fantastically, astride horses, mules, and donkeys (their own property), and cutting capers all over the Settlement.
crsnk10{_}1583{_}Two brass bands were out as well.
crsnk10{_}1584{_}Then there were the pa-u riders, thirty or forty of them, Hawaiian women all, superb horsewomen dressed gorgeously in the old, native riding costume, and dashing about in twos and threes and groups.
crsnk10{_}1585{_}In the afternoon Charmian and I stood in the judge's stand and awarded the prizes for horsemanship and costume to the pa-u riders.
crsnk10{_}1586{_}All about were the hundreds of lepers, with wreaths of flowers on heads and necks and shoulders, looking on and making merry.
crsnk10{_}1587{_}And always, over the brows of hills and across the grassy level stretches, appearing and disappearing, were the groups of men and women, gaily dressed, on galloping horses, horses and riders flower-bedecked and flower-garlanded, singing, and laughing, and riding like the wind.
crsnk10{_}1588{_}And as I stood in the judge's stand and looked at all this, there came to my recollection the lazar house of Havana, where I had once beheld some two hundred lepers, prisoners inside four restricted walls until they died.
crsnk10{_}1589{_}No, there are a few thousand places I wot of in this world over which I would select Molokai as a place of permanent residence.
crsnk10{_}1590{_}In the evening we went to one of the leper assembly halls, where, before a crowded audience, the singing societies contested for prizes, and where the night wound up with a dance.
crsnk10{_}1591{_}I have seen the Hawaiians living in the slums of Honolulu, and, having seen them, I can readily understand why the lepers, brought up from the Settlement for re-examination, shouted one and all, "Back to Molokai!"
crsnk10{_}1592{_}One thing is certain.
crsnk10{_}1593{_}The leper in the Settlement is far better off than the leper who lies in hiding outside.
crsnk10{_}1594{_}Such a leper is a lonely outcast, living in constant fear of discovery and slowly and surely rotting away.
crsnk10{_}1595{_}The action of leprosy is not steady.
crsnk10{_}1596{_}It lays hold of its victim, commits a ravage, and then lies dormant for an indeterminate period.
crsnk10{_}1597{_}It may not commit another ravage for five years, or ten years, or forty years, and the patient may enjoy uninterrupted good health.
crsnk10{_}1598{_}Rarely, however, do these first ravages cease of themselves.
crsnk10{_}1599{_}The skilled surgeon is required, and the skilled surgeon cannot be called in for the leper who is in hiding.
crsnk10{_}1600{_}For instance, the first ravage may take the form of a perforating ulcer in the sole of the foot.
crsnk10{_}1601{_}When the bone is reached, necrosis sets in.
crsnk10{_}1602{_}If the leper is in hiding, he cannot be operated upon, the necrosis will continue to eat its way up the bone of the leg, and in a brief and horrible time that leper will die of gangrene or some other terrible complication.
crsnk10{_}1603{_}On the other hand, if that same leper is in Molokai, the surgeon will operate upon the foot, remove the ulcer, cleanse the bone, and put a complete stop to that particular ravage of the disease.
crsnk10{_}1604{_}A month after the operation the leper will be out riding horseback, running foot races, swimming in the breakers, or climbing the giddy sides of the valleys for mountain apples.
crsnk10{_}1605{_}And as has been stated before, the disease, lying dormant, may not again attack him for five, ten, or forty years.
crsnk10{_}1606{_}The old horrors of leprosy go back to the conditions that obtained before the days of antiseptic surgery, and before the time when physicians like Dr. Goodhue and Dr. Hollmann went to live at the Settlement.
crsnk10{_}1607{_}Dr. Goodhue is the pioneer surgeon there, and too much praise cannot be given him for the noble work he has done.
crsnk10{_}1608{_}I spent one morning in the operating room with him and of the three operations he performed, two were on men, newcomers, who had arrived on the same steamer with me.
crsnk10{_}1609{_}In each case, the disease had attacked in one spot only.
crsnk10{_}1610{_}One had a perforating ulcer in the ankle, well advanced, and the other man was suffering from a similar affliction, well advanced, under his arm.
crsnk10{_}1611{_}Both cases were well advanced because the man had been on the outside and had not been treated.
crsnk10{_}1612{_}In each case.
crsnk10{_}1613{_}Dr. Goodhue put an immediate and complete stop to the ravage, and in four weeks those two men will be as well and able-bodied as they ever were in their lives.
crsnk10{_}1614{_}The only difference between them and you or me is that the disease is lying dormant in their bodies and may at any future time commit another ravage.
crsnk10{_}1615{_}Leprosy is as old as history.
crsnk10{_}1616{_}References to it are found in the earliest written records.
crsnk10{_}1617{_}And yet to-day practically nothing more is known about it than was known then.
crsnk10{_}1618{_}This much was known then, namely, that it was contagious and that those afflicted by it should be segregated.
crsnk10{_}1619{_}The difference between then and now is that to-day the leper is more rigidly segregated and more humanely treated.
crsnk10{_}1620{_}But leprosy itself still remains the same awful and profound mystery.
crsnk10{_}1621{_}A reading of the reports of the physicians and specialists of all countries reveals the baffling nature of the disease.
crsnk10{_}1622{_}These leprosy specialists are unanimous on no one phase of the disease.
crsnk10{_}1623{_}They do not know.
crsnk10{_}1624{_}In the past they rashly and dogmatically generalized.
crsnk10{_}1625{_}They generalize no longer.
crsnk10{_}1626{_}The one possible generalization that can be drawn from all the investigation that has been made is that leprosy is FEEBLY CONTAGIOUS.
crsnk10{_}1627{_}But in what manner it is feebly contagious is not known.
crsnk10{_}1628{_}They have isolated the bacillus of leprosy.
crsnk10{_}1629{_}They can determine by bacteriological examination whether or not a person is a leper; but they are as far away as ever from knowing how that bacillus finds its entrance into the body of a non- leper.
crsnk10{_}1630{_}They do not know the length of time of incubation.
crsnk10{_}1631{_}They have tried to inoculate all sorts of animals with leprosy, and have failed.
crsnk10{_}1632{_}They are baffled in the discovery of a serum wherewith to fight the disease.
crsnk10{_}1633{_}And in all their work, as yet, they have found no clue, no cure.
crsnk10{_}1634{_}Sometimes there have been blazes of hope, theories of causation and much heralded cures, but every time the darkness of failure quenched the flame.
crsnk10{_}1635{_}A doctor insists that the cause of leprosy is a long-continued fish diet, and he proves his theory voluminously till a physician from the highlands of India demands why the natives of that district should therefore be afflicted by leprosy when they have never eaten fish, nor all the generations of their fathers before them.
crsnk10{_}1636{_}A man treats a leper with a certain kind of oil or drug, announces a cure, and five, ten, or forty years afterwards the disease breaks out again.
crsnk10{_}1637{_}It is this trick of leprosy lying dormant in the body for indeterminate periods that is responsible for many alleged cures.
crsnk10{_}1638{_}But this much is certain:  AS YET THERE HAS BEEN NO AUTHENTIC CASE OF A CURE.
crsnk10{_}1639{_}Leprosy is FEEBLY CONTAGIOUS, but how is it contagious?
crsnk10{_}1640{_}An Austrian physician has inoculated himself and his assistants with leprosy and failed to catch it.
crsnk10{_}1641{_}But this is not conclusive, for there is the famous case of the Hawaiian murderer who had his sentence of death commuted to life imprisonment on his agreeing to be inoculated with the bacillus leprae.
crsnk10{_}1642{_}Some time after inoculation, leprosy made its appearance, and the man died a leper on Molokai.
crsnk10{_}1643{_}Nor was this conclusive, for it was discovered that at the time he was inoculated several members of his family were already suffering from the disease on Molokai.
crsnk10{_}1644{_}He may have contracted the disease from them, and it may have been well along in its mysterious period of incubation at the time he was officially inoculated.
crsnk10{_}1645{_}Then there is the case of that hero of the Church, Father Damien, who went to Molokai a clean man and died a leper.
crsnk10{_}1646{_}There have been many theories as to how he contracted leprosy, but nobody knows.
crsnk10{_}1647{_}He never knew himself.
crsnk10{_}1648{_}But every chance that he ran has certainly been run by a woman at present living in the Settlement; who has lived there many years; who has had five leper husbands, and had children by them; and who is to-day, as she always has been, free of the disease.
crsnk10{_}1649{_}As yet no light has been shed upon the mystery of leprosy.
crsnk10{_}1650{_}When more is learned about the disease, a cure for it may be expected.
crsnk10{_}1651{_}Once an efficacious serum is discovered, and leprosy, because it is so feebly contagious, will pass away swiftly from the earth.
crsnk10{_}1652{_}The battle waged with it will be short and sharp.
crsnk10{_}1653{_}In the meantime, how to discover that serum, or some other unguessed weapon?
crsnk10{_}1654{_}In the present it is a serious matter.
crsnk10{_}1655{_}It is estimated that there are half a million lepers, not segregated, in India alone.
crsnk10{_}1656{_}Carnegie libraries, Rockefeller universities, and many similar benefactions are all very well; but one cannot help thinking how far a few thousands of dollars would go, say in the leper Settlement of Molokai.
crsnk10{_}1657{_}The residents there are accidents of fate, scapegoats to some mysterious natural law of which man knows nothing, isolated for the welfare of their fellows who else might catch the dread disease, even as they have caught it, nobody knows how.
crsnk10{_}1658{_}Not for their sakes merely, but for the sake of future generations, a few thousands of dollars would go far in a legitimate and scientific search after a cure for leprosy, for a serum, or for some undreamed discovery that will enable the medical world to exterminate the bacillus leprae.
crsnk10{_}1659{_}There's the place for your money, you philanthropists.
crsnk10{_}1660{_}CHAPTER VIII--THE HOUSE OF THE SUN    There are hosts of people who journey like restless spirits round and about this earth in search of seascapes and landscapes and the wonders and beauties of nature.
crsnk10{_}1661{_}They overrun Europe in armies; they can be met in droves and herds in Florida and the West Indies, at the Pyramids, and on the slopes and summits of the Canadian and American Rockies; but in the House of the Sun they are as rare as live and wriggling dinosaurs.
crsnk10{_}1662{_}Haleakala is the Hawaiian name for "the House of the Sun."
crsnk10{_}1663{_}It is a noble dwelling, situated on the Island of Maui; but so few tourists have ever peeped into it, much less entered it, that their number may be practically reckoned as zero.
crsnk10{_}1664{_}Yet I venture to state that for natural beauty and wonder the nature-lover may see dissimilar things as great as Haleakala, but no greater, while he will never see elsewhere anything more beautiful or wonderful.
crsnk10{_}1665{_}Honolulu is six days' steaming from San Francisco; Maui is a night's run on the steamer from Honolulu; and six hours more if he is in a hurry, can bring the traveller to Kolikoli, which is ten thousand and thirty-two feet above the sea and which stands hard by the entrance portal to the House of the Sun.
crsnk10{_}1666{_}Yet the tourist comes not, and Haleakala sleeps on in lonely and unseen grandeur.
crsnk10{_}1667{_}Not being tourists, we of the Snark went to Haleakala.
crsnk10{_}1668{_}On the slopes of that monster mountain there is a cattle ranch of some fifty thousand acres, where we spent the night at an altitude of two thousand feet.
crsnk10{_}1669{_}The next morning it was boots and saddles, and with cow-boys and pack-horses we climbed to Ukulele, a mountain ranch- house, the altitude of which, fifty-five hundred feet, gives a severely temperate climate, compelling blankets at night and a roaring fireplace in the living-room.
crsnk10{_}1670{_}Ukulele, by the way, is the Hawaiian for "jumping flea" as it is also the Hawaiian for a certain musical instrument that may be likened to a young guitar.
crsnk10{_}1671{_}It is my opinion that the mountain ranch-house was named after the young guitar.
crsnk10{_}1672{_}We were not in a hurry, and we spent the day at Ukulele, learnedly discussing altitudes and barometers and shaking our particular barometer whenever any one's argument stood in need of demonstration.
crsnk10{_}1673{_}Our barometer was the most graciously acquiescent instrument I have ever seen.
crsnk10{_}1674{_}Also, we gathered mountain raspberries, large as hen's eggs and larger, gazed up the pasture- covered lava slopes to the summit of Haleakala, forty-five hundred feet above us, and looked down upon a mighty battle of the clouds that was being fought beneath us, ourselves in the bright sunshine.
crsnk10{_}1675{_}Every day and every day this unending battle goes on.
crsnk10{_}1676{_}Ukiukiu is the name of the trade-wind that comes raging down out of the north- east and hurls itself upon Haleakala.
crsnk10{_}1677{_}Now Haleakala is so bulky and tall that it turns the north-east trade-wind aside on either hand, so that in the lee of Haleakala no trade-wind blows at all.
crsnk10{_}1678{_}On the contrary, the wind blows in the counter direction, in the teeth of the north-east trade.
crsnk10{_}1679{_}This wind is called Naulu.
crsnk10{_}1680{_}And day and night and always Ukiukiu and Naulu strive with each other, advancing, retreating, flanking, curving, curling, and turning and twisting, the conflict made visible by the cloud-masses plucked from the heavens and hurled back and forth in squadrons, battalions, armies, and great mountain ranges.
crsnk10{_}1681{_}Once in a while, Ukiukiu, in mighty gusts, flings immense cloud-masses clear over the summit of Haleakala; whereupon Naulu craftily captures them, lines them up in new battle-formation, and with them smites back at his ancient and eternal antagonist.
crsnk10{_}1682{_}Then Ukiukiu sends a great cloud-army around the eastern-side of the mountain.
crsnk10{_}1683{_}It is a flanking movement, well executed.
crsnk10{_}1684{_}But Naulu, from his lair on the leeward side, gathers the flanking army in, pulling and twisting and dragging it, hammering it into shape, and sends it charging back against Ukiukiu around the western side of the mountain.
crsnk10{_}1685{_}And all the while, above and below the main battle-field, high up the slopes toward the sea, Ukiukiu and Naulu are continually sending out little wisps of cloud, in ragged skirmish line, that creep and crawl over the ground, among the trees and through the canyons, and that spring upon and capture one another in sudden ambuscades and sorties.
crsnk10{_}1686{_}And sometimes Ukiukiu or Naulu, abruptly sending out a heavy charging column, captures the ragged little skirmishers or drives them skyward, turning over and over, in vertical whirls, thousands of feet in the air.
crsnk10{_}1687{_}But it is on the western slopes of Haleakala that the main battle goes on.
crsnk10{_}1688{_}Here Naulu masses his heaviest formations and wins his greatest victories.
crsnk10{_}1689{_}Ukiukiu grows weak toward late afternoon, which is the way of all trade-winds, and is driven backward by Naulu.
crsnk10{_}1690{_}Naulu's generalship is excellent.
crsnk10{_}1691{_}All day he has been gathering and packing away immense reserves.
crsnk10{_}1692{_}As the afternoon draws on, he welds them into a solid column, sharp-pointed, miles in length, a mile in width, and hundreds of feet thick.
crsnk10{_}1693{_}This column he slowly thrusts forward into the broad battle-front of Ukiukiu, and slowly and surely Ukiukiu, weakening fast, is split asunder.
crsnk10{_}1694{_}But it is not all bloodless.
crsnk10{_}1695{_}At times Ukiukiu struggles wildly, and with fresh accessions of strength from the limitless north-east, smashes away half a mile at a time of Naulu's column and sweeps it off and away toward West Maui.
crsnk10{_}1696{_}Sometimes, when the two charging armies meet end- on, a tremendous perpendicular whirl results, the cloud-masses, locked together, mounting thousands of feet into the air and turning over and over.
crsnk10{_}1697{_}A favourite device of Ukiukiu is to send a low, squat formation, densely packed, forward along the ground and under Naulu.
crsnk10{_}1698{_}When Ukiukiu is under, he proceeds to buck.
crsnk10{_}1699{_}Naulu's mighty middle gives to the blow and bends upward, but usually he turns the attacking column back upon itself and sets it milling.
crsnk10{_}1700{_}And all the while the ragged little skirmishers, stray and detached, sneak through the trees and canyons, crawl along and through the grass, and surprise one another with unexpected leaps and rushes; while above, far above, serene and lonely in the rays of the setting sun, Haleakala looks down upon the conflict.
crsnk10{_}1701{_}And so, the night.
crsnk10{_}1702{_}But in the morning, after the fashion of trade-winds, Ukiukiu gathers strength and sends the hosts of Naulu rolling back in confusion and rout.
crsnk10{_}1703{_}And one day is like another day in the battle of the clouds, where Ukiukiu and Naulu strive eternally on the slopes of Haleakala.
crsnk10{_}1704{_}Again in the morning, it was boots and saddles, cow-boys, and packhorses, and the climb to the top began.
crsnk10{_}1705{_}One packhorse carried twenty gallons of water, slung in five-gallon bags on either side; for water is precious and rare in the crater itself, in spite of the fact that several miles to the north and east of the crater-rim more rain comes down than in any other place in the world.
crsnk10{_}1706{_}The way led upward across countless lava flows, without regard for trails, and never have I seen horses with such perfect footing as that of the thirteen that composed our outfit.
crsnk10{_}1707{_}They climbed or dropped down perpendicular places with the sureness and coolness of mountain goats, and never a horse fell or baulked.
crsnk10{_}1708{_}There is a familiar and strange illusion experienced by all who climb isolated mountains.
crsnk10{_}1709{_}The higher one climbs, the more of the earth's surface becomes visible, and the effect of this is that the horizon seems up-hill from the observer.
crsnk10{_}1710{_}This illusion is especially notable on Haleakala, for the old volcano rises directly from the sea without buttresses or connecting ranges.
crsnk10{_}1711{_}In consequence, as fast as we climbed up the grim slope of Haleakala, still faster did Haleakala, ourselves, and all about us, sink down into the centre of what appeared a profound abyss.
crsnk10{_}1712{_}Everywhere, far above us, towered the horizon.
crsnk10{_}1713{_}The ocean sloped down from the horizon to us.
crsnk10{_}1714{_}The higher we climbed, the deeper did we seem to sink down, the farther above us shone the horizon, and the steeper pitched the grade up to that horizontal line where sky and ocean met.
crsnk10{_}1715{_}It was weird and unreal, and vagrant thoughts of Simm's Hole and of the volcano through which Jules Verne journeyed to the centre of the earth flitted through one's mind.
crsnk10{_}1716{_}And then, when at last we reached the summit of that monster mountain, which summit was like the bottom of an inverted cone situated in the centre of an awful cosmic pit, we found that we were at neither top nor bottom.
crsnk10{_}1717{_}Far above us was the heaven-towering horizon, and far beneath us, where the top of the mountain should have been, was a deeper deep, the great crater, the House of the Sun.
crsnk10{_}1718{_}Twenty-three miles around stretched the dizzy wells of the crater.
crsnk10{_}1719{_}We stood on the edge of the nearly vertical western wall, and the floor of the crater lay nearly half a mile beneath.
crsnk10{_}1720{_}This floor, broken by lava-flows and cinder-cones, was as red and fresh and uneroded as if it were but yesterday that the fires went out.
crsnk10{_}1721{_}The cinder-cones, the smallest over four hundred feet in height and the largest over nine hundred, seemed no more than puny little sand- hills, so mighty was the magnitude of the setting.
crsnk10{_}1722{_}Two gaps, thousands of feet deep, broke the rim of the crater, and through these Ukiukiu vainly strove to drive his fleecy herds of trade-wind clouds.
crsnk10{_}1723{_}As fast as they advanced through the gaps, the heat of the crater dissipated them into thin air, and though they advanced always, they got nowhere.
crsnk10{_}1724{_}It was a scene of vast bleakness and desolation, stern, forbidding, fascinating.
crsnk10{_}1725{_}We gazed down upon a place of fire and earthquake.
crsnk10{_}1726{_}The tie-ribs of earth lay bare before us.
crsnk10{_}1727{_}It was a workshop of nature still cluttered with the raw beginnings of world-making.
crsnk10{_}1728{_}Here and there great dikes of primordial rock had thrust themselves up from the bowels of earth, straight through the molten surface- ferment that had evidently cooled only the other day.
crsnk10{_}1729{_}It was all unreal and unbelievable.
crsnk10{_}1730{_}Looking upward, far above us (in reality beneath us) floated the cloud-battle of Ukiukiu and Naulu.
crsnk10{_}1731{_}And higher up the slope of the seeming abyss, above the cloud-battle, in the air and sky, hung the islands of Lanai and Molokai.
crsnk10{_}1732{_}Across the crater, to the south-east, still apparently looking upward, we saw ascending, first, the turquoise sea, then the white surf-line of the shore of Hawaii; above that the belt of trade-clouds, and next, eighty miles away, rearing their stupendous hulks out of the azure sky, tipped with snow, wreathed with cloud, trembling like a mirage, the peaks of Mauna Kea and Mauna Loa hung poised on the wall of heaven.
crsnk10{_}1733{_}It is told that long ago, one Maui, the son of Hina, lived on what is now known as West Maui.
crsnk10{_}1734{_}His mother, Hina, employed her time in the making of kapas.
crsnk10{_}1735{_}She must have made them at night, for her days were occupied in trying to dry the kapas.
crsnk10{_}1736{_}Each morning, and all morning, she toiled at spreading them out in the sun.
crsnk10{_}1737{_}But no sooner were they out, than she began taking them in, in order to have them all under shelter for the night.
crsnk10{_}1738{_}For know that the days were shorter then than now.
crsnk10{_}1739{_}Maui watched his mother's futile toil and felt sorry for her.
crsnk10{_}1740{_}He decided to do something--oh, no, not to help her hang out and take in the kapas.
crsnk10{_}1741{_}He was too clever for that.
crsnk10{_}1742{_}His idea was to make the sun go slower.
crsnk10{_}1743{_}Perhaps he was the first Hawaiian astronomer.
crsnk10{_}1744{_}At any rate, he took a series of observations of the sun from various parts of the island.
crsnk10{_}1745{_}His conclusion was that the sun's path was directly across Haleakala.
crsnk10{_}1746{_}Unlike Joshua, he stood in no need of divine assistance.
crsnk10{_}1747{_}He gathered a huge quantity of coconuts, from the fibre of which he braided a stout cord, and in one end of which he made a noose, even as the cow-boys of Haleakala do to this day.
crsnk10{_}1748{_}Next he climbed into the House of the Sun and laid in wait.
crsnk10{_}1749{_}When the sun came tearing along the path, bent on completing its journey in the shortest time possible, the valiant youth threw his lariat around one of the sun's largest and strongest beams.
crsnk10{_}1750{_}He made the sun slow down some; also, he broke the beam short off.
crsnk10{_}1751{_}And he kept on roping and breaking off beams till the sun said it was willing to listen to reason.
crsnk10{_}1752{_}Maui set forth his terms of peace, which the sun accepted, agreeing to go more slowly thereafter.
crsnk10{_}1753{_}Wherefore Hina had ample time in which to dry her kapas, and the days are longer than they used to be, which last is quite in accord with the teachings of modern astronomy.
crsnk10{_}1754{_}We had a lunch of jerked beef and hard poi in a stone corral, used of old time for the night-impounding of cattle being driven across the island.
crsnk10{_}1755{_}Then we skirted the rim for half a mile and began the descent into the crater.
crsnk10{_}1756{_}Twenty-five hundred feet beneath lay the floor, and down a steep slope of loose volcanic cinders we dropped, the sure-footed horses slipping and sliding, but always keeping their feet.
crsnk10{_}1757{_}The black surface of the cinders, when broken by the horses' hoofs, turned to a yellow ochre dust, virulent in appearance and acid of taste, that arose in clouds.
crsnk10{_}1758{_}There was a gallop across a level stretch to the mouth of a convenient blow-hole, and then the descent continued in clouds of volcanic dust, winding in and out among cinder-cones, brick-red, old rose, and purplish black of colour.
crsnk10{_}1759{_}Above us, higher and higher, towered the crater-walls, while we journeyed on across innumerable lava-flows, turning and twisting a devious way among the adamantine billows of a petrified sea.
crsnk10{_}1760{_}Saw-toothed waves of lava vexed the surface of this weird ocean, while on either hand arose jagged crests and spiracles of fantastic shape.
crsnk10{_}1761{_}Our way led on past a bottomless pit and along and over the main stream of the latest lava-flow for seven miles.
crsnk10{_}1762{_}At the lower end of the crater was our camping spot, in a small grove of olapa and kolea trees, tucked away in a corner of the crater at the base of walls that rose perpendicularly fifteen hundred feet.
crsnk10{_}1763{_}Here was pasturage for the horses, but no water, and first we turned aside and picked our way across a mile of lava to a known water-hole in a crevice in the crater-wall.
crsnk10{_}1764{_}The water-hole was empty.
crsnk10{_}1765{_}But on climbing fifty feet up the crevice, a pool was found containing half a dozen barrels of water.
crsnk10{_}1766{_}A pail was carried up, and soon a steady stream of the precious liquid was running down the rock and filling the lower pool, while the cow-boys below were busy fighting the horses back, for there was room for one only to drink at a time.
crsnk10{_}1767{_}Then it was on to camp at the foot of the wall, up which herds of wild goats scrambled and blatted, while the tent arose to the sound of rifle-firing.
crsnk10{_}1768{_}Jerked beef, hard poi, and broiled kid were the menu.
crsnk10{_}1769{_}Over the crest of the crater, just above our heads, rolled a sea of clouds, driven on by Ukiukiu.
crsnk10{_}1770{_}Though this sea rolled over the crest unceasingly, it never blotted out nor dimmed the moon, for the heat of the crater dissolved the clouds as fast as they rolled in.
crsnk10{_}1771{_}Through the moonlight, attracted by the camp-fire, came the crater cattle to peer and challenge.
crsnk10{_}1772{_}They were rolling fat, though they rarely drank water, the morning dew on the grass taking its place.
crsnk10{_}1773{_}It was because of this dew that the tent made a welcome bedchamber, and we fell asleep to the chanting of hulas by the unwearied Hawaiian cowboys, in whose veins, no doubt, ran the blood of Maui, their valiant forebear.
crsnk10{_}1774{_}The camera cannot do justice to the House of the Sun.
crsnk10{_}1775{_}The sublimated chemistry of photography may not lie, but it certainly does not tell all the truth.
crsnk10{_}1776{_}The Koolau Gap may be faithfully reproduced, just as it impinged on the retina of the camera, yet in the resulting picture the gigantic scale of things would be missing.
crsnk10{_}1777{_}Those walls that seem several hundred feet in height are almost as many thousand; that entering wedge of cloud is a mile and a half wide in the gap itself, while beyond the gap it is a veritable ocean; and that foreground of cinder-cone and volcanic ash, mushy and colourless in appearance, is in truth gorgeous-hued in brick- red, terra-cotta rose, yellow ochre, and purplish black.
crsnk10{_}1778{_}Also, words are a vain thing and drive to despair.
crsnk10{_}1779{_}To say that a crater- wall is two thousand feet high is to say just precisely that it is two thousand feet high; but there is a vast deal more to that crater-wall than a mere statistic.
crsnk10{_}1780{_}The sun is ninety-three millions of miles distant, but to mortal conception the adjoining county is farther away.
crsnk10{_}1781{_}This frailty of the human brain is hard on the sun.
crsnk10{_}1782{_}It is likewise hard on the House of the Sun.
crsnk10{_}1783{_}Haleakala has a message of beauty and wonder for the human soul that cannot be delivered by proxy.
crsnk10{_}1784{_}Kolikoli is six hours from Kahului; Kahului is a night's run from Honolulu; Honolulu is six days from San Francisco; and there you are.
crsnk10{_}1785{_}We climbed the crater-walls, put the horses over impossible places, rolled stones, and shot wild goats.
crsnk10{_}1786{_}I did not get any goats.
crsnk10{_}1787{_}I was too busy rolling stones.
crsnk10{_}1788{_}One spot in particular I remember, where we started a stone the size of a horse.
crsnk10{_}1789{_}It began the descent easy enough, rolling over, wobbling, and threatening to stop; but in a few minutes it was soaring through the air two hundred feet at a jump.
crsnk10{_}1790{_}It grew rapidly smaller until it struck a slight slope of volcanic sand, over which it darted like a startled jackrabbit, kicking up behind it a tiny trail of yellow dust.
crsnk10{_}1791{_}Stone and dust diminished in size, until some of the party said the stone had stopped.
crsnk10{_}1792{_}That was because they could not see it any longer.
crsnk10{_}1793{_}It had vanished into the distance beyond their ken.
crsnk10{_}1794{_}Others saw it rolling farther on--I know I did; and it is my firm conviction that that stone is still rolling.
crsnk10{_}1795{_}Our last day in the crater, Ukiukiu gave us a taste of his strength.
crsnk10{_}1796{_}He smashed Naulu back all along the line, filled the House of the Sun to overflowing with clouds, and drowned us out.
crsnk10{_}1797{_}Our rain-gauge was a pint cup under a tiny hole in the tent.
crsnk10{_}1798{_}That last night of storm and rain filled the cup, and there was no way of measuring the water that spilled over into the blankets.
crsnk10{_}1799{_}With the rain-gauge out of business there was no longer any reason for remaining; so we broke camp in the wet-gray of dawn, and plunged eastward across the lava to the Kaupo Gap.
crsnk10{_}1800{_}East Maui is nothing more or less than the vast lava stream that flowed long ago through the Kaupo Gap; and down this stream we picked our way from an altitude of six thousand five hundred feet to the sea.
crsnk10{_}1801{_}This was a day's work in itself for the horses; but never were there such horses.
crsnk10{_}1802{_}Safe in the bad places, never rushing, never losing their heads, as soon as they found a trail wide and smooth enough to run on, they ran.
crsnk10{_}1803{_}There was no stopping them until the trail became bad again, and then they stopped of themselves.
crsnk10{_}1804{_}Continuously, for days, they had performed the hardest kind of work, and fed most of the time on grass foraged by themselves at night while we slept, and yet that day they covered twenty-eight leg-breaking miles and galloped into Hana like a bunch of colts.
crsnk10{_}1805{_}Also, there were several of them, reared in the dry region on the leeward side of Haleakala, that had never worn shoes in all their lives.
crsnk10{_}1806{_}Day after day, and all day long, unshod, they had travelled over the sharp lava, with the extra weight of a man on their backs, and their hoofs were in better condition than those of the shod horses.
crsnk10{_}1807{_}The scenery between Vieiras's (where the Kaupo Gap empties into the sea) and Lana, which we covered in half a day, is well worth a week or month; but, wildly beautiful as it is, it becomes pale and small in comparison with the wonderland that lies beyond the rubber plantations between Hana and the Honomanu Gulch.
crsnk10{_}1808{_}Two days were required to cover this marvellous stretch, which lies on the windward side of Haleakala.
crsnk10{_}1809{_}The people who dwell there call it the "ditch country," an unprepossessing name, but it has no other.
crsnk10{_}1810{_}Nobody else ever comes there.
crsnk10{_}1811{_}Nobody else knows anything about it.
crsnk10{_}1812{_}With the exception of a handful of men, whom business has brought there, nobody has heard of the ditch country of Maui.
crsnk10{_}1813{_}Now a ditch is a ditch, assumably muddy, and usually traversing uninteresting and monotonous landscapes.
crsnk10{_}1814{_}But the Nahiku Ditch is not an ordinary ditch.
crsnk10{_}1815{_}The windward side of Haleakala is serried by a thousand precipitous gorges, down which rush as many torrents, each torrent of which achieves a score of cascades and waterfalls before it reaches the sea.
crsnk10{_}1816{_}More rain comes down here than in any other region in the world.
crsnk10{_}1817{_}In 1904 the year's downpour was four hundred and twenty inches.
crsnk10{_}1818{_}Water means sugar, and sugar is the backbone of the territory of Hawaii, wherefore the Nahiku Ditch, which is not a ditch, but a chain of tunnels.
crsnk10{_}1819{_}The water travels underground, appearing only at intervals to leap a gorge, travelling high in the air on a giddy flume and plunging into and through the opposing mountain.
crsnk10{_}1820{_}This magnificent waterway is called a "ditch," and with equal appropriateness can Cleopatra's barge be called a box-car.
crsnk10{_}1821{_}There are no carriage roads through the ditch country, and before the ditch was built, or bored, rather, there was no horse-trail.
crsnk10{_}1822{_}Hundreds of inches of rain annually, on fertile soil, under a tropic sun, means a steaming jungle of vegetation.
crsnk10{_}1823{_}A man, on foot, cutting his way through, might advance a mile a day, but at the end of a week he would be a wreck, and he would have to crawl hastily back if he wanted to get out before the vegetation overran the passage way he had cut.
crsnk10{_}1824{_}O'Shaughnessy was the daring engineer who conquered the jungle and the gorges, ran the ditch and made the horse-trail.
crsnk10{_}1825{_}He built enduringly, in concrete and masonry, and made one of the most remarkable water-farms in the world.
crsnk10{_}1826{_}Every little runlet and dribble is harvested and conveyed by subterranean channels to the main ditch.
crsnk10{_}1827{_}But so heavily does it rain at times that countless spillways let the surplus escape to the sea.
crsnk10{_}1828{_}The horse-trail is not very wide.
crsnk10{_}1829{_}Like the engineer who built it, it dares anything.
crsnk10{_}1830{_}Where the ditch plunges through the mountain, it climbs over; and where the ditch leaps a gorge on a flume, the horse-trail takes advantage of the ditch and crosses on top of the flume.
crsnk10{_}1831{_}That careless trail thinks nothing of travelling up or down the faces of precipices.
crsnk10{_}1832{_}It gouges its narrow way out of the wall, dodging around waterfalls or passing under them where they thunder down in white fury; while straight overhead the wall rises hundreds of feet, and straight beneath it sinks a thousand.
crsnk10{_}1833{_}And those marvellous mountain horses are as unconcerned as the trail.
crsnk10{_}1834{_}They fox-trot along it as a matter of course, though the footing is slippery with rain, and they will gallop with their hind feet slipping over the edge if you let them.
crsnk10{_}1835{_}I advise only those with steady nerves and cool heads to tackle the Nahiku Ditch trail.
crsnk10{_}1836{_}One of our cow-boys was noted as the strongest and bravest on the big ranch.
crsnk10{_}1837{_}He had ridden mountain horses all his life on the rugged western slopes of Haleakala.
crsnk10{_}1838{_}He was first in the horse-breaking; and when the others hung back, as a matter of course, he would go in to meet a wild bull in the cattle-pen.
crsnk10{_}1839{_}He had a reputation.
crsnk10{_}1840{_}But he had never ridden over the Nahiku Ditch.
crsnk10{_}1841{_}It was there he lost his reputation.
crsnk10{_}1842{_}When he faced the first flume, spanning a hair-raising gorge, narrow, without railings, with a bellowing waterfall above, another below, and directly beneath a wild cascade, the air filled with driving spray and rocking to the clamour and rush of sound and motion--well, that cow-boy dismounted from his horse, explained briefly that he had a wife and two children, and crossed over on foot, leading the horse behind him.
crsnk10{_}1843{_}The only relief from the flumes was the precipices; and the only relief from the precipices was the flumes, except where the ditch was far under ground, in which case we crossed one horse and rider at a time, on primitive log-bridges that swayed and teetered and threatened to carry away.
crsnk10{_}1844{_}I confess that at first I rode such places with my feet loose in the stirrups, and that on the sheer walls I saw to it, by a definite, conscious act of will, that the foot in the outside stirrup, overhanging the thousand feet of fall, was exceedingly loose.
crsnk10{_}1845{_}I say "at first"; for, as in the crater itself we quickly lost our conception of magnitude, so, on the Nahiku Ditch, we quickly lost our apprehension of depth.
crsnk10{_}1846{_}The ceaseless iteration of height and depth produced a state of consciousness in which height and depth were accepted as the ordinary conditions of existence; and from the horse's back to look sheer down four hundred or five hundred feet became quite commonplace and non-productive of thrills.
crsnk10{_}1847{_}And as carelessly as the trail and the horses, we swung along the dizzy heights and ducked around or through the waterfalls.
crsnk10{_}1848{_}And such a ride!
crsnk10{_}1849{_}Falling water was everywhere.
crsnk10{_}1850{_}We rode above the clouds, under the clouds, and through the clouds!
crsnk10{_}1851{_}and every now and then a shaft of sunshine penetrated like a search-light to the depths yawning beneath us, or flashed upon some pinnacle of the crater-rim thousands of feet above.
crsnk10{_}1852{_}At every turn of the trail a waterfall or a dozen waterfalls, leaping hundreds of feet through the air, burst upon our vision.
crsnk10{_}1853{_}At our first night's camp, in the Keanae Gulch, we counted thirty-two waterfalls from a single viewpoint.
crsnk10{_}1854{_}The vegetation ran riot over that wild land.
crsnk10{_}1855{_}There were forests of koa and kolea trees, and candlenut trees; and then there were the trees called ohia-ai, which bore red mountain apples, mellow and juicy and most excellent to eat.
crsnk10{_}1856{_}Wild bananas grew everywhere, clinging to the sides of the gorges, and, overborne by their great bunches of ripe fruit, falling across the trail and blocking the way.
crsnk10{_}1857{_}And over the forest surged a sea of green life, the climbers of a thousand varieties, some that floated airily, in lacelike filaments, from the tallest branches others that coiled and wound about the trees like huge serpents; and one, the ei-ei, that was for all the world like a climbing palm, swinging on a thick stem from branch to branch and tree to tree and throttling the supports whereby it climbed.
crsnk10{_}1858{_}Through the sea of green, lofty tree-ferns thrust their great delicate fronds, and the lehua flaunted its scarlet blossoms.
crsnk10{_}1859{_}Underneath the climbers, in no less profusion, grew the warm-coloured, strangely-marked plants that in the United States one is accustomed to seeing preciously conserved in hot- houses.
crsnk10{_}1860{_}In fact, the ditch country of Maui is nothing more nor less than a huge conservatory.
crsnk10{_}1861{_}Every familiar variety of fern flourishes, and more varieties that are unfamiliar, from the tiniest maidenhair to the gross and voracious staghorn, the latter the terror of the woodsmen, interlacing with itself in tangled masses five or six feet deep and covering acres.
crsnk10{_}1862{_}Never was there such a ride.
crsnk10{_}1863{_}For two days it lasted, when we emerged into rolling country, and, along an actual wagon-road, came home to the ranch at a gallop.
crsnk10{_}1864{_}I know it was cruel to gallop the horses after such a long, hard journey; but we blistered our hands in vain effort to hold them in.
crsnk10{_}1865{_}That's the sort of horses they grow on Haleakala.
crsnk10{_}1866{_}At the ranch there was great festival of cattle- driving, branding, and horse-breaking.
crsnk10{_}1867{_}Overhead Ukiukiu and Naulu battled valiantly, and far above, in the sunshine, towered the mighty summit of Haleakala.
crsnk10{_}1868{_}CHAPTER IX--A PACIFIC TRAVERSE    Sandwich Islands to Tahiti.--There is great difficulty in making this passage across the trades.
crsnk10{_}1869{_}The whalers and all others speak with great doubt of fetching Tahiti from the Sandwich islands.
crsnk10{_}1870{_}Capt.
crsnk10{_}1871{_}Bruce says that a vessel should keep to the northward until she gets a start of wind before bearing for her destination.
crsnk10{_}1872{_}In his passage between them in November, 1837, he had no variables near the line in coming south, and never could make easting on either tack, though he endeavoured by every means to do so.
crsnk10{_}1873{_}So say the sailing directions for the South Pacific Ocean; and that is all they say.
crsnk10{_}1874{_}There is not a word more to help the weary voyager in making this long traverse--nor is there any word at all concerning the passage from Hawaii to the Marquesas, which lie some eight hundred miles to the northeast of Tahiti and which are the more difficult to reach by just that much.
crsnk10{_}1875{_}The reason for the lack of directions is, I imagine, that no voyager is supposed to make himself weary by attempting so impossible a traverse.
crsnk10{_}1876{_}But the impossible did not deter the Snark,--principally because of the fact that we did not read that particular little paragraph in the sailing directions until after we had started.
crsnk10{_}1877{_}We sailed from Hilo, Hawaii, on October 7, and arrived at Nuka-hiva, in the Marquesas, on December 6.
crsnk10{_}1878{_}The distance was two thousand miles as the crow flies, while we actually travelled at least four thousand miles to accomplish it, thus proving for once and for ever that the shortest distance between two points is not always a straight line.
crsnk10{_}1879{_}Had we headed directly for the Marquesas, we might have travelled five or six thousand miles.
crsnk10{_}1880{_}Upon one thing we were resolved:  we would not cross the Line west of 130 degrees west longitude.
crsnk10{_}1881{_}For here was the problem.
crsnk10{_}1882{_}To cross the Line to the west of that point, if the southeast trades were well around to the southeast, would throw us so far to leeward of the Marquesas that a head-beat would be maddeningly impossible.
crsnk10{_}1883{_}Also, we had to remember the equatorial current, which moves west at a rate of anywhere from twelve to seventy-five miles a day.
crsnk10{_}1884{_}A pretty pickle, indeed, to be to leeward of our destination with such a current in our teeth.
crsnk10{_}1885{_}No; not a minute, nor a second, west of 130 degrees west longitude would we cross the Line.
crsnk10{_}1886{_}But since the southeast trades were to be expected five or six degrees north of the Line (which, if they were well around to the southeast or south- southeast, would necessitate our sliding off toward south- southwest), we should have to hold to the eastward, north of the Line, and north of the southeast trades, until we gained at least 128 degrees west longitude.
crsnk10{_}1887{_}I have forgotten to mention that the seventy-horse-power gasolene engine, as usual, was not working, and that we could depend upon wind alone.
crsnk10{_}1888{_}Neither was the launch engine working.
crsnk10{_}1889{_}And while I am about it, I may as well confess that the five-horse-power, which ran the lights, fans, and pumps, was also on the sick-list.
crsnk10{_}1890{_}A striking title for a book haunts me, waking and sleeping.
crsnk10{_}1891{_}I should like to write that book some day and to call it "Around the World with Three Gasolene Engines and a Wife."
crsnk10{_}1892{_}But I am afraid I shall not write it, for fear of hurting the feelings of some of the young gentlemen of San Francisco, Honolulu, and Hilo, who learned their trades at the expense of the Snark's engines.
crsnk10{_}1893{_}It looked easy on paper.
crsnk10{_}1894{_}Here was Hilo and there was our objective, 128 degrees west longitude.
crsnk10{_}1895{_}With the northeast trade blowing we could travel a straight line between the two points, and even slack our sheets off a goodly bit.
crsnk10{_}1896{_}But one of the chief troubles with the trades is that one never knows just where he will pick them up and just in what direction they will be blowing.
crsnk10{_}1897{_}We picked up the northeast trade right outside of Hilo harbour, but the miserable breeze was away around into the east.
crsnk10{_}1898{_}Then there was the north equatorial current setting westward like a mighty river.
crsnk10{_}1899{_}Furthermore, a small boat, by the wind and bucking into a big headsea, does not work to advantage.
crsnk10{_}1900{_}She jogs up and down and gets nowhere.
crsnk10{_}1901{_}Her sails are full and straining, every little while she presses her lee-rail under, she flounders, and bumps, and splashes, and that is all.
crsnk10{_}1902{_}Whenever she begins to gather way, she runs ker- chug into a big mountain of water and is brought to a standstill.
crsnk10{_}1903{_}So, with the Snark, the resultant of her smallness, of the trade around into the east, and of the strong equatorial current, was a long sag south.
crsnk10{_}1904{_}Oh, she did not go quite south.
crsnk10{_}1905{_}But the easting she made was distressing.
crsnk10{_}1906{_}On October 11, she made forty miles easting; October 12, fifteen miles; October 13, no easting; October 14, thirty miles; October 15, twenty-three miles; October 16, eleven miles; and on October 17, she actually went to the westward four miles.
crsnk10{_}1907{_}Thus, in a week she made one hundred and fifteen miles easting, which was equivalent to sixteen miles a day.
crsnk10{_}1908{_}But, between the longitude of Hilo and 128 degrees west longitude is a difference of twenty-seven degrees, or, roughly, sixteen hundred miles.
crsnk10{_}1909{_}At sixteen miles a day, one hundred days would be required to accomplish this distance.
crsnk10{_}1910{_}And even then, our objective, l28 degrees west longitude, was five degrees north of the Line, while Nuka-hiva, in the Marquesas, lay nine degrees south of the Line and twelve degrees to the west!
crsnk10{_}1911{_}There remained only one thing to do--to work south out of the trade and into the variables.
crsnk10{_}1912{_}It is true that Captain Bruce found no variables on his traverse, and that he "never could make easting on either tack."
crsnk10{_}1913{_}It was the variables or nothing with us, and we prayed for better luck than he had had.
crsnk10{_}1914{_}The variables constitute the belt of ocean lying between the trades and the doldrums, and are conjectured to be the draughts of heated air which rise in the doldrums, flow high in the air counter to the trades, and gradually sink down till they fan the surface of the ocean where they are found.
crsnk10{_}1915{_}And they are found where they are found; for they are wedged between the trades and the doldrums, which same shift their territory from day to day and month to month.
crsnk10{_}1916{_}We found the variables in 11 degrees north latitude, and 11 degrees north latitude we hugged jealously.
crsnk10{_}1917{_}To the south lay the doldrums.
crsnk10{_}1918{_}To the north lay the northeast trade that refused to blow from the northeast.
crsnk10{_}1919{_}The days came and went, and always they found the Snark somewhere near the eleventh parallel.
crsnk10{_}1920{_}The variables were truly variable.
crsnk10{_}1921{_}A light head-wind would die away and leave us rolling in a calm for forty-eight hours.
crsnk10{_}1922{_}Then a light head-wind would spring up, blow for three hours, and leave us rolling in another calm for forty-eight hours.
crsnk10{_}1923{_}Then--hurrah!--the wind would come out of the west, fresh, beautifully fresh, and send the Snark along, wing and wing, her wake bubbling, the log-line straight astern.
crsnk10{_}1924{_}At the end of half an hour, while we were preparing to set the spinnaker, with a few sickly gasps the wind would die away.
crsnk10{_}1925{_}And so it went.
crsnk10{_}1926{_}We wagered optimistically on every favourable fan of air that lasted over five minutes; but it never did any good.
crsnk10{_}1927{_}The fans faded out just the same.
crsnk10{_}1928{_}But there were exceptions.
crsnk10{_}1929{_}In the variables, if you wait long enough, something is bound to happen, and we were so plentifully stocked with food and water that we could afford to wait.
crsnk10{_}1930{_}On October 26, we actually made one hundred and three miles of easting, and we talked about it for days afterwards.
crsnk10{_}1931{_}Once we caught a moderate gale from the south, which blew itself out in eight hours, but it helped us to seventy-one miles of easting in that particular twenty-four hours.
crsnk10{_}1932{_}And then, just as it was expiring, the wind came straight out from the north (the directly opposite quarter), and fanned us along over another degree of easting.
crsnk10{_}1933{_}In years and years no sailing vessel has attempted this traverse, and we found ourselves in the midst of one of the loneliest of the Pacific solitudes.
crsnk10{_}1934{_}In the sixty days we were crossing it we sighted no sail, lifted no steamer's smoke above the horizon.
crsnk10{_}1935{_}A disabled vessel could drift in this deserted expanse for a dozen generations, and there would be no rescue.
crsnk10{_}1936{_}The only chance of rescue would be from a vessel like the Snark, and the Snark happened to be there principally because of the fact that the traverse had been begun before the particular paragraph in the sailing directions had been read.
crsnk10{_}1937{_}Standing upright on deck, a straight line drawn from the eye to the horizon would measure three miles and a half.
crsnk10{_}1938{_}Thus, seven miles was the diameter of the circle of the sea in which we had our centre.
crsnk10{_}1939{_}Since we remained always in the centre, and since we constantly were moving in some direction, we looked upon many circles.
crsnk10{_}1940{_}But all circles looked alike.
crsnk10{_}1941{_}No tufted islets, gray headlands, nor glistening patches of white canvas ever marred the symmetry of that unbroken curve.
crsnk10{_}1942{_}Clouds came and went, rising up over the rim of the circle, flowing across the space of it, and spilling away and down across the opposite rim.
crsnk10{_}1943{_}The world faded as the procession of the weeks marched by.
crsnk10{_}1944{_}The world faded until at last there ceased to be any world except the little world of the Snark, freighted with her seven souls and floating on the expanse of the waters.
crsnk10{_}1945{_}Our memories of the world, the great world, became like dreams of former lives we had lived somewhere before we came to be born on the Snark.
crsnk10{_}1946{_}After we had been out of fresh vegetables for some time, we mentioned such things in much the same way I have heard my father mention the vanished apples of his boyhood.
crsnk10{_}1947{_}Man is a creature of habit, and we on the Snark had got the habit of the Snark.
crsnk10{_}1948{_}Everything about her and aboard her was as a matter of course, and anything different would have been an irritation and an offence.
crsnk10{_}1949{_}There was no way by which the great world could intrude.
crsnk10{_}1950{_}Our bell rang the hours, but no caller ever rang it.
crsnk10{_}1951{_}There were no guests to dinner, no telegrams, no insistent telephone jangles invading our privacy.
crsnk10{_}1952{_}We had no engagements to keep, no trains to catch, and there were no morning newspapers over which to waste time in learning what was happening to our fifteen hundred million other fellow-creatures.
crsnk10{_}1953{_}But it was not dull.
crsnk10{_}1954{_}The affairs of our little world had to be regulated, and, unlike the great world, our world had to be steered in its journey through space.
crsnk10{_}1955{_}Also, there were cosmic disturbances to be encountered and baffled, such as do not afflict the big earth in its frictionless orbit through the windless void.
crsnk10{_}1956{_}And we never knew, from moment to moment, what was going to happen next.
crsnk10{_}1957{_}There were spice and variety enough and to spare.
crsnk10{_}1958{_}Thus, at four in the morning, I relieve Hermann at the wheel.
crsnk10{_}1959{_}"East-northeast," he gives me the course.
crsnk10{_}1960{_}"She's eight points off, but she ain't steering."
crsnk10{_}1961{_}Small wonder.
crsnk10{_}1962{_}The vessel does not exist that can be steered in so absolute a calm.
crsnk10{_}1963{_}"I had a breeze a little while ago--maybe it will come back again," Hermann says hopefully, ere he starts forward to the cabin and his bunk.
crsnk10{_}1964{_}The mizzen is in and fast furled.
crsnk10{_}1965{_}In the night, what of the roll and the absence of wind, it had made life too hideous to be permitted to go on rasping at the mast, smashing at the tackles, and buffeting the empty air into hollow outbursts of sound.
crsnk10{_}1966{_}But the big mainsail is still on, and the staysail, jib, and flying-jib are snapping and slashing at their sheets with every roll.
crsnk10{_}1967{_}Every star is out.
crsnk10{_}1968{_}Just for luck I put the wheel hard over in the opposite direction to which it had been left by Hermann, and I lean back and gaze up at the stars.
crsnk10{_}1969{_}There is nothing else for me to do.
crsnk10{_}1970{_}There is nothing to be done with a sailing vessel rolling in a stark calm.
crsnk10{_}1971{_}Then I feel a fan on my cheek, faint, so faint, that I can just sense it ere it is gone.
crsnk10{_}1972{_}But another comes, and another, until a real and just perceptible breeze is blowing.
crsnk10{_}1973{_}How the Snark's sails manage to feel it is beyond me, but feel it they do, as she does as well, for the compass card begins slowly to revolve in the binnacle.
crsnk10{_}1974{_}In reality, it is not revolving at all.
crsnk10{_}1975{_}It is held by terrestrial magnetism in one place, and it is the Snark that is revolving, pivoted upon that delicate cardboard device that floats in a closed vessel of alcohol.
crsnk10{_}1976{_}So the Snark comes back on her course.
crsnk10{_}1977{_}The breath increases to a tiny puff.
crsnk10{_}1978{_}The Snark feels the weight of it and actually heels over a trifle.
crsnk10{_}1979{_}There is flying scud overhead, and I notice the stars being blotted out.
crsnk10{_}1980{_}Walls of darkness close in upon me, so that, when the last star is gone, the darkness is so near that it seems I can reach out and touch it on every side.
crsnk10{_}1981{_}When I lean toward it, I can feel it loom against my face.
crsnk10{_}1982{_}Puff follows puff, and I am glad the mizzen is furled.
crsnk10{_}1983{_}Phew!
crsnk10{_}1984{_}that was a stiff one!
crsnk10{_}1985{_}The Snark goes over and down until her lee-rail is buried and the whole Pacific Ocean is pouring in.
crsnk10{_}1986{_}Four or five of these gusts make me wish that the jib and flying-jib were in.
crsnk10{_}1987{_}The sea is picking up, the gusts are growing stronger and more frequent, and there is a splatter of wet in the air.
crsnk10{_}1988{_}There is no use in attempting to gaze to windward.
crsnk10{_}1989{_}The wall of blackness is within arm's length.
crsnk10{_}1990{_}Yet I cannot help attempting to see and gauge the blows that are being struck at the Snark.
crsnk10{_}1991{_}There is something ominous and menacing up there to windward, and I have a feeling that if I look long enough and strong enough, I shall divine it.
crsnk10{_}1992{_}Futile feeling.
crsnk10{_}1993{_}Between two gusts I leave the wheel and run forward to the cabin companionway, where I light matches and consult the barometer.
crsnk10{_}1994{_}"29-90" it reads.
crsnk10{_}1995{_}That sensitive instrument refuses to take notice of the disturbance which is humming with a deep, throaty voice in the rigging.
crsnk10{_}1996{_}I get back to the wheel just in time to meet another gust, the strongest yet.
crsnk10{_}1997{_}Well, anyway, the wind is abeam and the Snark is on her course, eating up easting.
crsnk10{_}1998{_}That at least is well.
crsnk10{_}1999{_}The jib and flying-jib bother me, and I wish they were in.
crsnk10{_}2000{_}She would make easier weather of it, and less risky weather likewise.
crsnk10{_}2001{_}The wind snorts, and stray raindrops pelt like birdshot.
crsnk10{_}2002{_}I shall certainly have to call all hands, I conclude; then conclude the next instant to hang on a little longer.
crsnk10{_}2003{_}Maybe this is the end of it, and I shall have called them for nothing.
crsnk10{_}2004{_}It is better to let them sleep.
crsnk10{_}2005{_}I hold the Snark down to her task, and from out of the darkness, at right angles, comes a deluge of rain accompanied by shrieking wind.
crsnk10{_}2006{_}Then everything eases except the blackness, and I rejoice in that I have not called the men.
crsnk10{_}2007{_}No sooner does the wind ease than the sea picks up.
crsnk10{_}2008{_}The combers are breaking now, and the boat is tossing like a cork.
crsnk10{_}2009{_}Then out of the blackness the gusts come harder and faster than before.
crsnk10{_}2010{_}If only I knew what was up there to windward in the blackness!
crsnk10{_}2011{_}The Snark is making heavy weather of it, and her lee-rail is buried oftener than not.
crsnk10{_}2012{_}More shrieks and snorts of wind.
crsnk10{_}2013{_}Now, if ever, is the time to call the men.
crsnk10{_}2014{_}I WILL call them, I resolve.
crsnk10{_}2015{_}Then there is a burst of rain, a slackening of the wind, and I do not call.
crsnk10{_}2016{_}But it is rather lonely, there at the wheel, steering a little world through howling blackness.
crsnk10{_}2017{_}It is quite a responsibility to be all alone on the surface of a little world in time of stress, doing the thinking for its sleeping inhabitants.
crsnk10{_}2018{_}I recoil from the responsibility as more gusts begin to strike and as a sea licks along the weather rail and splashes over into the cockpit.
crsnk10{_}2019{_}The salt water seems strangely warm to my body and is shot through with ghostly nodules of phosphorescent light.
crsnk10{_}2020{_}I shall surely call all hands to shorten sail.
crsnk10{_}2021{_}Why should they sleep?
crsnk10{_}2022{_}I am a fool to have any compunctions in the matter.
crsnk10{_}2023{_}My intellect is arrayed against my heart.
crsnk10{_}2024{_}It was my heart that said, "Let them sleep."
crsnk10{_}2025{_}Yes, but it was my intellect that backed up my heart in that judgment.
crsnk10{_}2026{_}Let my intellect then reverse the judgment; and, while I am speculating as to what particular entity issued that command to my intellect, the gusts die away.
crsnk10{_}2027{_}Solicitude for mere bodily comfort has no place in practical seamanship, I conclude sagely; but study the feel of the next series of gusts and do not call the men.
crsnk10{_}2028{_}After all, it IS my intellect, behind everything, procrastinating, measuring its knowledge of what the Snark can endure against the blows being struck at her, and waiting the call of all hands against the striking of still severer blows.
crsnk10{_}2029{_}Daylight, gray and violent, steals through the cloud-pall and shows a foaming sea that flattens under the weight of recurrent and increasing squalls.
crsnk10{_}2030{_}Then comes the rain, filling the windy valleys of the sea with milky smoke and further flattening the waves, which but wait for the easement of wind and rain to leap more wildly than before.
crsnk10{_}2031{_}Come the men on deck, their sleep out, and among them Hermann, his face on the broad grin in appreciation of the breeze of wind I have picked up.
crsnk10{_}2032{_}I turn the wheel over to Warren and start to go below, pausing on the way to rescue the galley stovepipe which has gone adrift.
crsnk10{_}2033{_}I am barefooted, and my toes have had an excellent education in the art of clinging; but, as the rail buries itself in a green sea, I suddenly sit down on the streaming deck.
crsnk10{_}2034{_}Hermann good-naturedly elects to question my selection of such a spot.
crsnk10{_}2035{_}Then comes the next roll, and he sits down, suddenly, and without premeditation.
crsnk10{_}2036{_}The Snark heels over and down, the rail takes it green, and Hermann and I, clutching the precious stove-pipe, are swept down into the lee-scuppers.
crsnk10{_}2037{_}After that I finish my journey below, and while changing my clothes grin with satisfaction--the Snark is making easting.
crsnk10{_}2038{_}No, it is not all monotony.
crsnk10{_}2039{_}When we had worried along our easting to 126 degrees west longitude, we left the variables and headed south through the doldrums, where was much calm weather and where, taking advantage of every fan of air, we were often glad to make a score of miles in as many hours.
crsnk10{_}2040{_}And yet, on such a day, we might pass through a dozen squalls and be surrounded by dozens more.
crsnk10{_}2041{_}And every squall was to be regarded as a bludgeon capable of crushing the Snark.
crsnk10{_}2042{_}We were struck sometimes by the centres and sometimes by the sides of these squalls, and we never knew just where or how we were to be hit.
crsnk10{_}2043{_}The squall that rose up, covering half the heavens, and swept down upon us, as likely as not split into two squalls which passed us harmlessly on either side while the tiny, innocent looking squall that appeared to carry no more than a hogshead of water and a pound of wind, would abruptly assume cyclopean proportions, deluging us with rain and overwhelming us with wind.
crsnk10{_}2044{_}Then there were treacherous squalls that went boldly astern and sneaked back upon us from a mile to leeward.
crsnk10{_}2045{_}Again, two squalls would tear along, one on each side of us, and we would get a fillip from each of them.
crsnk10{_}2046{_}Now a gale certainly grows tiresome after a few hours, but squalls never.
crsnk10{_}2047{_}The thousandth squall in one's experience is as interesting as the first one, and perhaps a bit more so.
crsnk10{_}2048{_}It is the tyro who has no apprehension of them.
crsnk10{_}2049{_}The man of a thousand squalls respects a squall.
crsnk10{_}2050{_}He knows what they are.
crsnk10{_}2051{_}It was in the doldrums that our most exciting event occurred.
crsnk10{_}2052{_}On November 20, we discovered that through an accident we had lost over one-half of the supply of fresh water that remained to us.
crsnk10{_}2053{_}Since we were at that time forty-three days out from Hilo, our supply of fresh water was not large.
crsnk10{_}2054{_}To lose over half of it was a catastrophe.
crsnk10{_}2055{_}On close allowance, the remnant of water we possessed would last twenty days.
crsnk10{_}2056{_}But we were in the doldrums; there was no telling where the southeast trades were, nor where we would pick them up.
crsnk10{_}2057{_}The handcuffs were promptly put upon the pump, and once a day the water was portioned out.
crsnk10{_}2058{_}Each of us received a quart for personal use, and eight quarts were given to the cook.
crsnk10{_}2059{_}Enters now the psychology of the situation.
crsnk10{_}2060{_}No sooner had the discovery of the water shortage been made than I, for one, was afflicted with a burning thirst.
crsnk10{_}2061{_}It seemed to me that I had never been so thirsty in my life.
crsnk10{_}2062{_}My little quart of water I could easily have drunk in one draught, and to refrain from doing so required a severe exertion of will.
crsnk10{_}2063{_}Nor was I alone in this.
crsnk10{_}2064{_}All of us talked water, thought water, and dreamed water when we slept.
crsnk10{_}2065{_}We examined the charts for possible islands to which to run in extremity, but there were no such islands.
crsnk10{_}2066{_}The Marquesas were the nearest, and they were the other side of the Line, and of the doldrums, too, which made it even worse.
crsnk10{_}2067{_}We were in 3 degrees north latitude, while the Marquesas were 9 degrees south latitude--a difference of over a thousand miles.
crsnk10{_}2068{_}Furthermore, the Marquesas lay some fourteen degrees to the west of our longitude.
crsnk10{_}2069{_}A pretty pickle for a handful of creatures sweltering on the ocean in the heat of tropic calms.
crsnk10{_}2070{_}We rigged lines on either side between the main and mizzen riggings.
crsnk10{_}2071{_}To these we laced the big deck awning, hoisting it up aft with a sailing pennant so that any rain it might collect would run forward where it could be caught.
crsnk10{_}2072{_}Here and there squalls passed across the circle of the sea.
crsnk10{_}2073{_}All day we watched them, now to port or starboard, and again ahead or astern.
crsnk10{_}2074{_}But never one came near enough to wet us.
crsnk10{_}2075{_}In the afternoon a big one bore down upon us.
crsnk10{_}2076{_}It spread out across the ocean as it approached, and we could see it emptying countless thousands of gallons into the salt sea.
crsnk10{_}2077{_}Extra attention was paid to the awning and then we waited.
crsnk10{_}2078{_}Warren, Martin, and Hermann made a vivid picture.
crsnk10{_}2079{_}Grouped together, holding on to the rigging, swaying to the roll, they were gazing intently at the squall.
crsnk10{_}2080{_}Strain, anxiety, and yearning were in every posture of their bodies.
crsnk10{_}2081{_}Beside them was the dry and empty awning.
crsnk10{_}2082{_}But they seemed to grow limp and to droop as the squall broke in half, one part passing on ahead, the other drawing astern and going to leeward.
crsnk10{_}2083{_}But that night came rain.
crsnk10{_}2084{_}Martin, whose psychological thirst had compelled him to drink his quart of water early, got his mouth down to the lip of the awning and drank the deepest draught I ever have seen drunk.
crsnk10{_}2085{_}The precious water came down in bucketfuls and tubfuls, and in two hours we caught and stored away in the tanks one hundred and twenty gallons.
crsnk10{_}2086{_}Strange to say, in all the rest of our voyage to the Marquesas not another drop of rain fell on board.
crsnk10{_}2087{_}If that squall had missed us, the handcuffs would have remained on the pump, and we would have busied ourselves with utilizing our surplus gasolene for distillation purposes.
crsnk10{_}2088{_}Then there was the fishing.
crsnk10{_}2089{_}One did not have to go in search of it, for it was there at the rail.
crsnk10{_}2090{_}A three-inch steel hook, on the end of a stout line, with a piece of white rag for bait, was all that was necessary to catch bonitas weighing from ten to twenty-five pounds.
crsnk10{_}2091{_}Bonitas feed on flying-fish, wherefore they are unaccustomed to nibbling at the hook.
crsnk10{_}2092{_}They strike as gamely as the gamest fish in the sea, and their first run is something that no man who has ever caught them will forget.
crsnk10{_}2093{_}Also, bonitas are the veriest cannibals.
crsnk10{_}2094{_}The instant one is hooked he is attacked by his fellows.
crsnk10{_}2095{_}Often and often we hauled them on board with fresh, clean-bitten holes in them the size of teacups.
crsnk10{_}2096{_}One school of bonitas, numbering many thousands, stayed with us day and night for more than three weeks.
crsnk10{_}2097{_}Aided by the Snark, it was great hunting; for they cut a swath of destruction through the ocean half a mile wide and fifteen hundred miles in length.
crsnk10{_}2098{_}They ranged along abreast of the Snark on either side, pouncing upon the flying- fish her forefoot scared up.
crsnk10{_}2099{_}Since they were continually pursuing astern the flying-fish that survived for several flights, they were always overtaking the Snark, and at any time one could glance astern and on the front of a breaking wave see scores of their silvery forms coasting down just under the surface.
crsnk10{_}2100{_}When they had eaten their fill, it was their delight to get in the shadow of the boat, or of her sails, and a hundred or so were always to be seen lazily sliding along and keeping cool.
crsnk10{_}2101{_}But the poor flying-fish!
crsnk10{_}2102{_}Pursued and eaten alive by the bonitas and dolphins, they sought flight in the air, where the swooping seabirds drove them back into the water.
crsnk10{_}2103{_}Under heaven there was no refuge for them.
crsnk10{_}2104{_}Flying-fish do not play when they essay the air.
crsnk10{_}2105{_}It is a life-and-death affair with them.
crsnk10{_}2106{_}A thousand times a day we could lift our eyes and see the tragedy played out.
crsnk10{_}2107{_}The swift, broken circling of a guny might attract one's attention.
crsnk10{_}2108{_}A glance beneath shows the back of a dolphin breaking the surface in a wild rush.
crsnk10{_}2109{_}Just in front of its nose a shimmering palpitant streak of silver shoots from the water into the air--a delicate, organic mechanism of flight, endowed with sensation, power of direction, and love of life.
crsnk10{_}2110{_}The guny swoops for it and misses, and the flying- fish, gaining its altitude by rising, kite-like, against the wind, turns in a half-circle and skims off to leeward, gliding on the bosom of the wind.
crsnk10{_}2111{_}Beneath it, the wake of the dolphin shows in churning foam.
crsnk10{_}2112{_}So he follows, gazing upward with large eyes at the flashing breakfast that navigates an element other than his own.
crsnk10{_}2113{_}He cannot rise to so lofty occasion, but he is a thorough-going empiricist, and he knows, sooner or later, if not gobbled up by the guny, that the flying-fish must return to the water.
crsnk10{_}2114{_}And then-- breakfast.
crsnk10{_}2115{_}We used to pity the poor winged fish.
crsnk10{_}2116{_}It was sad to see such sordid and bloody slaughter.
crsnk10{_}2117{_}And then, in the night watches, when a forlorn little flying-fish struck the mainsail and fell gasping and splattering on the deck, we would rush for it just as eagerly, just as greedily, just as voraciously, as the dolphins and bonitas.
crsnk10{_}2118{_}For know that flying-fish are most toothsome for breakfast.
crsnk10{_}2119{_}It is always a wonder to me that such dainty meat does not build dainty tissue in the bodies of the devourers.
crsnk10{_}2120{_}Perhaps the dolphins and bonitas are coarser-fibred because of the high speed at which they drive their bodies in order to catch their prey.
crsnk10{_}2121{_}But then again, the flying-fish drive their bodies at high speed, too.
crsnk10{_}2122{_}Sharks we caught occasionally, on large hooks, with chain-swivels, bent on a length of small rope.
crsnk10{_}2123{_}And sharks meant pilot-fish, and remoras, and various sorts of parasitic creatures.
crsnk10{_}2124{_}Regular man- eaters some of the sharks proved, tiger-eyed and with twelve rows of teeth, razor-sharp.
crsnk10{_}2125{_}By the way, we of the Snark are agreed that we have eaten many fish that will not compare with baked shark smothered in tomato dressing.
crsnk10{_}2126{_}In the calms we occasionally caught a fish called "hake" by the Japanese cook.
crsnk10{_}2127{_}And once, on a spoon-hook trolling a hundred yards astern, we caught a snake-like fish, over three feet in length and not more than three inches in diameter, with four fangs in his jaw.
crsnk10{_}2128{_}He proved the most delicious fish-- delicious in meat and flavour--that we have ever eaten on board.
crsnk10{_}2129{_}The most welcome addition to our larder was a green sea-turtle, weighing a full hundred pounds and appearing on the table most appetizingly in steaks, soups, and stews, and finally in a wonderful curry which tempted all hands into eating more rice than was good for them.
crsnk10{_}2130{_}The turtle was sighted to windward, calmly sleeping on the surface in the midst of a huge school of curious dolphins.
crsnk10{_}2131{_}It was a deep-sea turtle of a surety, for the nearest land was a thousand miles away.
crsnk10{_}2132{_}We put the Snark about and went back for him, Hermann driving the granes into his head and neck.
