crsnk10{_}2842{_}The huge Pacific seas, extending in unbroken lines sometimes a mile or half as much again in length, hurl themselves upon the reef, overtowering and falling upon it with tremendous crashes, and yet the fragile coral structure withstands the shock and protects the land.
crsnk10{_}2843{_}Outside lies destruction to the mightiest ship afloat.
crsnk10{_}2844{_}Inside reigns the calm of untroubled water, whereon a canoe like ours can sail with no more than a couple of inches of free-board.
crsnk10{_}2845{_}We flew over the water.
crsnk10{_}2846{_}And such water!--clear as the clearest spring-water, and crystalline in its clearness, all intershot with a maddening pageant of colours and rainbow ribbons more magnificently gorgeous than any rainbow.
crsnk10{_}2847{_}Jade green alternated with turquoise, peacock blue with emerald, while now the canoe skimmed over reddish purple pools, and again over pools of dazzling, shimmering white where pounded coral sand lay beneath and upon which oozed monstrous sea-slugs.
crsnk10{_}2848{_}One moment we were above wonder-gardens of coral, wherein coloured fishes disported, fluttering like marine butterflies; the next moment we were dashing across the dark surface of deep channels, out of which schools of flying fish lifted their silvery flight; and a third moment we were above other gardens of living coral, each more wonderful than the last.
crsnk10{_}2849{_}And above all was the tropic, trade-wind sky with its fluffy clouds racing across the zenith and heaping the horizon with their soft masses.
crsnk10{_}2850{_}Before we were aware, we were close in to Tahaa (pronounced Tah-hah- ah, with equal accents), and Tehei was grinning approval of the vahine's proficiency at bailing.
crsnk10{_}2851{_}The canoe grounded on a shallow shore, twenty feet from land, and we waded out on a soft bottom where big slugs curled and writhed under our feet and where small octopuses advertised their existence by their superlative softness when stepped upon.
crsnk10{_}2852{_}Close to the beach, amid cocoanut palms and banana trees, erected on stilts, built of bamboo, with a grass- thatched roof, was Tehei's house.
crsnk10{_}2853{_}And out of the house came Tehei's vahine, a slender mite of a woman, kindly eyed and Mongolian of feature--when she was not North American Indian.
crsnk10{_}2854{_}"Bihaura," Tehei called her, but he did not pronounce it according to English notions of spelling.
crsnk10{_}2855{_}Spelled "Bihaura," it sounded like Bee-ah-oo-rah, with every syllable sharply emphasized.
crsnk10{_}2856{_}She took Charmian by the hand and led her into the house, leaving Tehei and me to follow.
crsnk10{_}2857{_}Here, by sign-language unmistakable, we were informed that all they possessed was ours.
crsnk10{_}2858{_}No hidalgo was ever more generous in the expression of giving, while I am sure that few hidalgos were ever as generous in the actual practice.
crsnk10{_}2859{_}We quickly discovered that we dare not admire their possessions, for whenever we did admire a particular object it was immediately presented to us.
crsnk10{_}2860{_}The two vahines, according to the way of vahines, got together in a discussion and examination of feminine fripperies, while Tehei and I, manlike, went over fishing-tackle and wild-pig-hunting, to say nothing of the device whereby bonitas are caught on forty-foot poles from double canoes.
crsnk10{_}2861{_}Charmian admired a sewing basket--the best example she had seen of Polynesian basketry; it was hers.
crsnk10{_}2862{_}I admired a bonita hook, carved in one piece from a pearl-shell; it was mine.
crsnk10{_}2863{_}Charmian was attracted by a fancy braid of straw sennit, thirty feet of it in a roll, sufficient to make a hat of any design one wished; the roll of sennit was hers.
crsnk10{_}2864{_}My gaze lingered upon a poi-pounder that dated back to the old stone days; it was mine.
crsnk10{_}2865{_}Charmian dwelt a moment too long on a wooden poi-bowl, canoe-shaped, with four legs, all carved in one piece of wood; it was hers.
crsnk10{_}2866{_}I glanced a second time at a gigantic cocoanut calabash; it was mine.
crsnk10{_}2867{_}Then Charmian and I held a conference in which we resolved to admire no more--not because it did not pay well enough, but because it paid too well.
crsnk10{_}2868{_}Also, we were already racking our brains over the contents of the Snark for suitable return presents.
crsnk10{_}2869{_}Christmas is an easy problem compared with a Polynesian giving-feast.
crsnk10{_}2870{_}We sat on the cool porch, on Bihaura's best mats while dinner was preparing, and at the same time met the villagers.
crsnk10{_}2871{_}In twos and threes and groups they strayed along, shaking hands and uttering the Tahitian word of greeting--Ioarana, pronounced yo-rah-nah.
crsnk10{_}2872{_}The men, big strapping fellows, were in loin-cloths, with here and there no shirt, while the women wore the universal ahu, a sort of adult pinafore that flows in graceful lines from the shoulders to the ground.
crsnk10{_}2873{_}Sad to see was the elephantiasis that afflicted some of them.
crsnk10{_}2874{_}Here would be a comely woman of magnificent proportions, with the port of a queen, yet marred by one arm four times--or a dozen times--the size of the other.
crsnk10{_}2875{_}Beside her might stand a six-foot man, erect, mighty-muscled, bronzed, with the body of a god, yet with feet and calves so swollen that they ran together, forming legs, shapeless, monstrous, that were for all the world like elephant legs.
crsnk10{_}2876{_}No one seems really to know the cause of the South Sea elephantiasis.
crsnk10{_}2877{_}One theory is that it is caused by the drinking of polluted water.
crsnk10{_}2878{_}Another theory attributes it to inoculation through mosquito bites.
crsnk10{_}2879{_}A third theory charges it to predisposition plus the process of acclimatization.
crsnk10{_}2880{_}On the other hand, no one that stands in finicky dread of it and similar diseases can afford to travel in the South Seas.
crsnk10{_}2881{_}There will be occasions when such a one must drink water.
crsnk10{_}2882{_}There may be also occasions when the mosquitoes let up biting.
crsnk10{_}2883{_}But every precaution of the finicky one will be useless.
crsnk10{_}2884{_}If he runs barefoot across the beach to have a swim, he will tread where an elephantiasis case trod a few minutes before.
crsnk10{_}2885{_}If he closets himself in his own house, yet every bit of fresh food on his table will have been subjected to the contamination, be it flesh, fish, fowl, or vegetable.
crsnk10{_}2886{_}In the public market at Papeete two known lepers run stalls, and heaven alone knows through what channels arrive at that market the daily supplies of fish, fruit, meat, and vegetables.
crsnk10{_}2887{_}The only happy way to go through the South Seas is with a careless poise, without apprehension, and with a Christian Science-like faith in the resplendent fortune of your own particular star.
crsnk10{_}2888{_}When you see a woman, afflicted with elephantiasis wringing out cream from cocoanut meat with her naked hands, drink and reflect how good is the cream, forgetting the hands that pressed it out.
crsnk10{_}2889{_}Also, remember that diseases such as elephantiasis and leprosy do not seem to be caught by contact.
crsnk10{_}2890{_}We watched a Raratongan woman, with swollen, distorted limbs, prepare our cocoanut cream, and then went out to the cook-shed where Tehei and Bihaura were cooking dinner.
crsnk10{_}2891{_}And then it was served to us on a dry-goods box in the house.
crsnk10{_}2892{_}Our hosts waited until we were done and then spread their table on the floor.
crsnk10{_}2893{_}But our table!
crsnk10{_}2894{_}We were certainly in the high seat of abundance.
crsnk10{_}2895{_}First, there was glorious raw fish, caught several hours before from the sea and steeped the intervening time in lime-juice diluted with water.
crsnk10{_}2896{_}Then came roast chicken.
crsnk10{_}2897{_}Two cocoanuts, sharply sweet, served for drink.
crsnk10{_}2898{_}There were bananas that tasted like strawberries and that melted in the mouth, and there was banana-poi that made one regret that his Yankee forebears ever attempted puddings.
crsnk10{_}2899{_}Then there was boiled yam, boiled taro, and roasted feis, which last are nothing more or less than large mealy, juicy, red-coloured cooking bananas.
crsnk10{_}2900{_}We marvelled at the abundance, and, even as we marvelled, a pig was brought on, a whole pig, a sucking pig, swathed in green leaves and roasted upon the hot stones of a native oven, the most honourable and triumphant dish in the Polynesian cuisine.
crsnk10{_}2901{_}And after that came coffee, black coffee, delicious coffee, native coffee grown on the hillsides of Tahaa.
crsnk10{_}2902{_}Tehei's fishing-tackle fascinated me, and after we arranged to go fishing, Charmian and I decided to remain all night.
crsnk10{_}2903{_}Again Tehei broached Samoa, and again my petit bateau brought the disappointment and the smile of acquiescence to his face.
crsnk10{_}2904{_}Bora Bora was my next port.
crsnk10{_}2905{_}It was not so far away but that cutters made the passage back and forth between it and Raiatea.
crsnk10{_}2906{_}So I invited Tehei to go that far with us on the Snark.
crsnk10{_}2907{_}Then I learned that his wife had been born on Bora Bora and still owned a house there.
crsnk10{_}2908{_}She likewise was invited, and immediately came the counter invitation to stay with them in their house in Born Bora.
crsnk10{_}2909{_}It was Monday.
crsnk10{_}2910{_}Tuesday we would go fishing and return to Raiatea.
crsnk10{_}2911{_}Wednesday we would sail by Tahaa and off a certain point, a mile away, pick up Tehei and Bihaura and go on to Bora Bora.
crsnk10{_}2912{_}All this we arranged in detail, and talked over scores of other things as well, and yet Tehei knew three phrases in English, Charmian and I knew possibly a dozen Tahitian words, and among the four of us there were a dozen or so French words that all understood.
crsnk10{_}2913{_}Of course, such polyglot conversation was slow, but, eked out with a pad, a lead pencil, the face of a clock Charmian drew on the back of a pad, and with ten thousand and one gestures, we managed to get on very nicely.
crsnk10{_}2914{_}At the first moment we evidenced an inclination for bed the visiting natives, with soft Iaoranas, faded away, and Tehei and Bihaura likewise faded away.
crsnk10{_}2915{_}The house consisted of one large room, and it was given over to us, our hosts going elsewhere to sleep.
crsnk10{_}2916{_}In truth, their castle was ours.
crsnk10{_}2917{_}And right here, I want to say that of all the entertainment I have received in this world at the hands of all sorts of races in all sorts of places, I have never received entertainment that equalled this at the hands of this brown-skinned couple of Tahaa.
crsnk10{_}2918{_}I do not refer to the presents, the free-handed generousness, the high abundance, but to the fineness of courtesy and consideration and tact, and to the sympathy that was real sympathy in that it was understanding.
crsnk10{_}2919{_}They did nothing they thought ought to be done for us, according to their standards, but they did what they divined we waited to be done for us, while their divination was most successful.
crsnk10{_}2920{_}It would be impossible to enumerate the hundreds of little acts of consideration they performed during the few days of our intercourse.
crsnk10{_}2921{_}Let it suffice for me to say that of all hospitality and entertainment I have known, in no case was theirs not only not excelled, but in no case was it quite equalled.
crsnk10{_}2922{_}Perhaps the most delightful feature of it was that it was due to no training, to no complex social ideals, but that it was the untutored and spontaneous outpouring from their hearts.
crsnk10{_}2923{_}The next morning we went fishing, that is, Tehei, Charmian, and I did, in the coffin-shaped canoe; but this time the enormous sail was left behind.
crsnk10{_}2924{_}There was no room for sailing and fishing at the same time in that tiny craft.
crsnk10{_}2925{_}Several miles away, inside the reef, in a channel twenty fathoms deep, Tehei dropped his baited hooks and rock-sinkers.
crsnk10{_}2926{_}The bait was chunks of octopus flesh, which he bit out of a live octopus that writhed in the bottom of the canoe.
crsnk10{_}2927{_}Nine of these lines he set, each line attached to one end of a short length of bamboo floating on the surface.
crsnk10{_}2928{_}When a fish was hooked, the end of the bamboo was drawn under the water.
crsnk10{_}2929{_}Naturally, the other end rose up in the air, bobbing and waving frantically for us to make haste.
crsnk10{_}2930{_}And make haste we did, with whoops and yells and driving paddles, from one signalling bamboo to another, hauling up from the depths great glistening beauties from two to three feet in length.
crsnk10{_}2931{_}Steadily, to the eastward, an ominous squall had been rising and blotting out the bright trade-wind sky.
crsnk10{_}2932{_}And we were three miles to leeward of home.
crsnk10{_}2933{_}We started as the first wind-gusts whitened the water.
crsnk10{_}2934{_}Then came the rain, such rain as only the tropics afford, where every tap and main in the sky is open wide, and when, to top it all, the very reservoir itself spills over in blinding deluge.
crsnk10{_}2935{_}Well, Charmian was in a swimming suit, I was in pyjamas, and Tehei wore only a loin-cloth.
crsnk10{_}2936{_}Bihaura was on the beach waiting for us, and she led Charmian into the house in much the same fashion that the mother leads in the naughty little girl who has been playing in mud-puddles.
crsnk10{_}2937{_}It was a change of clothes and a dry and quiet smoke while kai-kai was preparing.
crsnk10{_}2938{_}Kai-kai, by the way, is the Polynesian for "food" or "to eat," or, rather, it is one form of the original root, whatever it may have been, that has been distributed far and wide over the vast area of the Pacific.
crsnk10{_}2939{_}It is kai in the Marquesas, Raratonga, Manahiki, Niue, Fakaafo, Tonga, New Zealand, and Vate.
crsnk10{_}2940{_}In Tahiti "to eat" changes to amu, in Hawaii and Samoa to ai, in Ban to kana, in Nina to kana, in Nongone to kaka, and in New Caledonia to ki.
crsnk10{_}2941{_}But by whatsoever sound or symbol, it was welcome to our ears after that long paddle in the rain.
crsnk10{_}2942{_}Once more we sat in the high seat of abundance until we regretted that we had been made unlike the image of the giraffe and the camel.
crsnk10{_}2943{_}Again, when we were preparing to return to the Snark, the sky to windward turned black and another squall swooped down.
crsnk10{_}2944{_}But this time it was little rain and all wind.
crsnk10{_}2945{_}It blew hour after hour, moaning and screeching through the palms, tearing and wrenching and shaking the frail bamboo dwelling, while the outer reef set no a mighty thundering as it broke the force of the swinging seas.
crsnk10{_}2946{_}Inside the reef, the lagoon, sheltered though it was, was white with fury, and not even Tehei's seamanship could have enabled his slender canoe to live in such a welter.
crsnk10{_}2947{_}By sunset, the back of the squall had broken though it was still too rough for the canoe.
crsnk10{_}2948{_}So I had Tehei find a native who was willing to venture his cutter across to Raiatea for the outrageous sum of two dollars, Chili, which is equivalent in our money to ninety cents.
crsnk10{_}2949{_}Half the village was told off to carry presents, with which Tehei and Bihaura speeded their parting guests--captive chickens, fishes dressed and swathed in wrappings of green leaves, great golden bunches of bananas, leafy baskets spilling over with oranges and limes, alligator pears (the butter-fruit, also called the avoca), huge baskets of yams, bunches of taro and cocoanuts, and last of all, large branches and trunks of trees--firewood for the Snark.
crsnk10{_}2950{_}While on the way to the cutter we met the only white man on Tahaa, and of all men, George Lufkin, a native of New England!
crsnk10{_}2951{_}Eighty-six years of age he was, sixty-odd of which, he said, he had spent in the Society Islands, with occasional absences, such as the gold rush to Eldorado in 'forty-nine and a short period of ranching in California near Tulare.
crsnk10{_}2952{_}Given no more than three months by the doctors to live, he had returned to his South Seas and lived to eighty-six and to chuckle over the doctors aforesaid, who were all in their graves.
crsnk10{_}2953{_}Fee-fee he had, which is the native for elephantiasis and which is pronounced fay-fay.
crsnk10{_}2954{_}A quarter of a century before, the disease had fastened upon him, and it would remain with him until he died.
crsnk10{_}2955{_}We asked him about kith and kin.
crsnk10{_}2956{_}Beside him sat a sprightly damsel of sixty, his daughter.
crsnk10{_}2957{_}"She is all I have," he murmured plaintively, "and she has no children living."
crsnk10{_}2958{_}The cutter was a small, sloop-rigged affair, but large it seemed alongside Tehei's canoe.
crsnk10{_}2959{_}On the other hand, when we got out on the lagoon and were struck by another heavy wind-squall, the cutter became liliputian, while the Snark, in our imagination, seemed to promise all the stability and permanence of a continent.
crsnk10{_}2960{_}They were good boatmen.
crsnk10{_}2961{_}Tehei and Bihaura had come along to see us home, and the latter proved a good boatwoman herself.
crsnk10{_}2962{_}The cutter was well ballasted, and we met the squall under full sail.
crsnk10{_}2963{_}It was getting dark, the lagoon was full of coral patches, and we were carrying on.
crsnk10{_}2964{_}In the height of the squall we had to go about, in order to make a short leg to windward to pass around a patch of coral no more than a foot under the surface.
crsnk10{_}2965{_}As the cutter filled on the other tack, and while she was in that "dead" condition that precedes gathering way, she was knocked flat.
crsnk10{_}2966{_}Jib-sheet and main-sheet were let go, and she righted into the wind.
crsnk10{_}2967{_}Three times she was knocked down, and three times the sheets were flung loose, before she could get away on that tack.
crsnk10{_}2968{_}By the time we went about again, darkness had fallen.
crsnk10{_}2969{_}We were now to windward of the Snark, and the squall was howling.
crsnk10{_}2970{_}In came the jib, and down came the mainsail, all but a patch of it the size of a pillow-slip.
crsnk10{_}2971{_}By an accident we missed the Snark, which was riding it out to two anchors, and drove aground upon the inshore coral.
crsnk10{_}2972{_}Running the longest line on the Snark by means of the launch, and after an hour's hard work, we heaved the cutter off and had her lying safely astern.
crsnk10{_}2973{_}The day we sailed for Bora Bora the wind was light, and we crossed the lagoon under power to the point where Tehei and Bihaura were to meet us.
crsnk10{_}2974{_}As we made in to the land between the coral banks, we vainly scanned the shore for our friends.
crsnk10{_}2975{_}There was no sign of them.
crsnk10{_}2976{_}"We can't wait," I said.
crsnk10{_}2977{_}"This breeze won't fetch us to Bora Bora by dark, and I don't want to use any more gasolene than I have to."
crsnk10{_}2978{_}You see, gasolene in the South Seas is a problem.
crsnk10{_}2979{_}One never knows when he will be able to replenish his supply.
crsnk10{_}2980{_}But just then Tehei appeared through the trees as he came down to the water.
crsnk10{_}2981{_}He had peeled off his shirt and was wildly waving it.
crsnk10{_}2982{_}Bihaura apparently was not ready.
crsnk10{_}2983{_}Once aboard, Tehei informed us by signs that we must proceed along the land till we got opposite to his house.
crsnk10{_}2984{_}He took the wheel and conned the Snark through the coral, around point after point till we cleared the last point of all.
crsnk10{_}2985{_}Cries of welcome went up from the beach, and Bihaura, assisted by several of the villagers, brought off two canoe-loads of abundance.
crsnk10{_}2986{_}There were yams, taro, feis, breadfruit, cocoanuts, oranges, limes, pineapples, watermelons, alligator pears, pomegranates, fish, chickens galore crowing and cackling and laying eggs on our decks, and a live pig that squealed infernally and all the time in apprehension of imminent slaughter.
crsnk10{_}2987{_}Under the rising moon we came in through the perilous passage of the reef of Bora Bora and dropped anchor off Vaitape village.
crsnk10{_}2988{_}Bihaura, with housewifely anxiety, could not get ashore too quickly to her house to prepare more abundance for us.
crsnk10{_}2989{_}While the launch was taking her and Tehei to the little jetty, the sound of music and of singing drifted across the quiet lagoon.
crsnk10{_}2990{_}Throughout the Society Islands we had been continually informed that we would find the Bora Borans very jolly.
crsnk10{_}2991{_}Charmian and I went ashore to see, and on the village green, by forgotten graves on the beach, found the youths and maidens dancing, flower-garlanded and flower-bedecked, with strange phosphorescent flowers in their hair that pulsed and dimmed and glowed in the moonlight.
crsnk10{_}2992{_}Farther along the beach we came upon a huge grass house, oval-shaped seventy feet in length, where the elders of the village were singing himines.
crsnk10{_}2993{_}They, too, were flower- garlanded and jolly, and they welcomed us into the fold as little lost sheep straying along from outer darkness.
crsnk10{_}2994{_}Early next morning Tehei was on board, with a string of fresh-caught fish and an invitation to dinner for that evening.
crsnk10{_}2995{_}On the way to dinner, we dropped in at the himine house.
crsnk10{_}2996{_}The same elders were singing, with here or there a youth or maiden that we had not seen the previous night.
crsnk10{_}2997{_}From all the signs, a feast was in preparation.
crsnk10{_}2998{_}Towering up from the floor was a mountain of fruits and vegetables, flanked on either side by numerous chickens tethered by cocoanut strips.
crsnk10{_}2999{_}After several himines had been sung, one of the men arose and made oration.
crsnk10{_}3000{_}The oration was made to us, and though it was Greek to us, we knew that in some way it connected us with that mountain of provender.
crsnk10{_}3001{_}"Can it be that they are presenting us with all that?"
crsnk10{_}3002{_}Charmian whispered.
crsnk10{_}3003{_}"Impossible," I muttered back.
crsnk10{_}3004{_}"Why should they be giving it to us?
crsnk10{_}3005{_}Besides, there is no room on the Snark for it.
crsnk10{_}3006{_}We could not eat a tithe of it.
crsnk10{_}3007{_}The rest would spoil.
crsnk10{_}3008{_}Maybe they are inviting us to the feast.
crsnk10{_}3009{_}At any rate, that they should give all that to us is impossible."
crsnk10{_}3010{_}Nevertheless we found ourselves once more in the high seat of abundance.
crsnk10{_}3011{_}The orator, by gestures unmistakable, in detail presented every item in the mountain to us, and next he presented it to us in toto.
crsnk10{_}3012{_}It was an embarrassing moment.
crsnk10{_}3013{_}What would you do if you lived in a hall bedroom and a friend gave you a white elephant?
crsnk10{_}3014{_}Our Snark was no more than a hall bedroom, and already she was loaded down with the abundance of Tahaa.
crsnk10{_}3015{_}This new supply was too much.
crsnk10{_}3016{_}We blushed, and stammered, and mauruuru'd.
crsnk10{_}3017{_}We mauruuru'd with repeated nui's which conveyed the largeness and overwhelmingness of our thanks.
crsnk10{_}3018{_}At the same time, by signs, we committed the awful breach of etiquette of not accepting the present.
crsnk10{_}3019{_}The himine singers' disappointment was plainly betrayed, and that evening, aided by Tehei, we compromised by accepting one chicken, one bunch of bananas, one bunch of taro, and so on down the list.
crsnk10{_}3020{_}But there was no escaping the abundance.
crsnk10{_}3021{_}I bought a dozen chickens from a native out in the country, and the following day he delivered thirteen chickens along with a canoe-load of fruit.
crsnk10{_}3022{_}The French storekeeper presented us with pomegranates and lent us his finest horse.
crsnk10{_}3023{_}The gendarme did likewise, lending us a horse that was the very apple of his eye.
crsnk10{_}3024{_}And everybody sent us flowers.
crsnk10{_}3025{_}The Snark was a fruit-stand and a greengrocer's shop masquerading under the guise of a conservatory.
crsnk10{_}3026{_}We went around flower-garlanded all the time.
crsnk10{_}3027{_}When the himine singers came on board to sing, the maidens kissed us welcome, and the crew, from captain to cabin-boy, lost its heart to the maidens of Bora Bora.
crsnk10{_}3028{_}Tehei got up a big fishing expedition in our honour, to which we went in a double canoe, paddled by a dozen strapping Amazons.
crsnk10{_}3029{_}We were relieved that no fish were caught, else the Snark would have sunk at her moorings.
crsnk10{_}3030{_}The days passed, but the abundance did not diminish.
crsnk10{_}3031{_}On the day of departure, canoe after canoe put off to us.
crsnk10{_}3032{_}Tehei brought cucumbers and a young papaia tree burdened with splendid fruit.
crsnk10{_}3033{_}Also, for me he brought a tiny, double canoe with fishing apparatus complete.
crsnk10{_}3034{_}Further, he brought fruits and vegetables with the same lavishness as at Tahaa.
crsnk10{_}3035{_}Bihaura brought various special presents for Charmian, such as silk-cotton pillows, fans, and fancy mats.
crsnk10{_}3036{_}The whole population brought fruits, flowers, and chickens.
crsnk10{_}3037{_}And Bihaura added a live sucking pig.
crsnk10{_}3038{_}Natives whom I did not remember ever having seen before strayed over the rail and presented me with such things as fish-poles, fish-lines, and fish-hooks carved from pearl-shell.
crsnk10{_}3039{_}As the Snark sailed out through the reef, she had a cutter in tow.
crsnk10{_}3040{_}This was the craft that was to take Bihaura back to Tahaa--but not Tehei.
crsnk10{_}3041{_}I had yielded at last, and he was one of the crew of the Snark.
crsnk10{_}3042{_}When the cutter cast off and headed east, and the Snark's bow turned toward the west, Tehei knelt down by the cockpit and breathed a silent prayer, the tears flowing down his cheeks.
crsnk10{_}3043{_}A week later, when Martin got around to developing and printing, he showed Tehei some of the photographs.
crsnk10{_}3044{_}And that brown-skinned son of Polynesia, gazing on the pictured lineaments of his beloved Bihaura broke down in tears.
crsnk10{_}3045{_}But the abundance!
crsnk10{_}3046{_}There was so much of it.
crsnk10{_}3047{_}We could not work the Snark for the fruit that was in the way.
crsnk10{_}3048{_}She was festooned with fruit.
crsnk10{_}3049{_}The life-boat and launch were packed with it.
crsnk10{_}3050{_}The awning- guys groaned under their burdens.
crsnk10{_}3051{_}But once we struck the full trade-wind sea, the disburdening began.
crsnk10{_}3052{_}At every roll the Snark shook overboard a bunch or so of bananas and cocoanuts, or a basket of limes.
crsnk10{_}3053{_}A golden flood of limes washed about in the lee-scuppers.
crsnk10{_}3054{_}The big baskets of yams burst, and pineapples and pomegranates rolled back and forth.
crsnk10{_}3055{_}The chickens had got loose and were everywhere, roosting on the awnings, fluttering and squawking out on the jib-boom, and essaying the perilous feat of balancing on the spinnaker-boom.
crsnk10{_}3056{_}They were wild chickens, accustomed to flight.
crsnk10{_}3057{_}When attempts were made to catch them, they flew out over the ocean, circled about, and came lack.
crsnk10{_}3058{_}Sometimes they did not come back.
crsnk10{_}3059{_}And in the confusion, unobserved, the little sucking pig got loose and slipped overboard.
crsnk10{_}3060{_}"On the arrival of strangers, every man endeavoured to obtain one as a friend and carry him off to his own habitation, where he is treated with the greatest kindness by the inhabitants of the district:  they place him on a high seat and feed him with abundance of the finest foods."
crsnk10{_}3061{_}CHAPTER XIII--THE STONE-FISHING OF BORA BORA    At five in the morning the conches began to blow.
crsnk10{_}3062{_}From all along the beach the eerie sounds arose, like the ancient voice of War, calling to the fishermen to arise and prepare to go forth.
crsnk10{_}3063{_}We on the Snark likewise arose, for there could be no sleep in that mad din of conches.
crsnk10{_}3064{_}Also, we were going stone-fishing, though our preparations were few.
crsnk10{_}3065{_}Tautai-taora is the name for stone-fishing, tautai meaning a "fishing instrument."
crsnk10{_}3066{_}And taora meaning "thrown."
crsnk10{_}3067{_}But tautai- taora, in combination, means "stone-fishing," for a stone is the instrument that is thrown.
crsnk10{_}3068{_}Stone-fishing is in reality a fish- drive, similar in principle to a rabbit-drive or a cattle-drive, though in the latter affairs drivers and driven operate in the same medium, while in the fish-drive the men must be in the air to breathe and the fish are driven through the water.
crsnk10{_}3069{_}It does not matter if the water is a hundred feet deep, the men, working on the surface, drive the fish just the same.
crsnk10{_}3070{_}This is the way it is done.
crsnk10{_}3071{_}The canoes form in line, one hundred to two hundred feet apart.
crsnk10{_}3072{_}In the bow of each canoe a man wields a stone, several pounds in weight, which is attached to a short rope.
crsnk10{_}3073{_}He merely smites the water with the stone, pulls up the stone, and smites again.
crsnk10{_}3074{_}He goes on smiting.
crsnk10{_}3075{_}In the stern of each canoe another man paddles, driving the canoe ahead and at the same time keeping it in the formation.
crsnk10{_}3076{_}The line of canoes advances to meet a second line a mile or two away, the ends of the lines hurrying together to form a circle, the far edge of which is the shore.
crsnk10{_}3077{_}The circle begins to contract upon the shore, where the women, standing in a long row out into the sea, form a fence of legs, which serves to break any rushes of the frantic fish.
crsnk10{_}3078{_}At the right moment when the circle is sufficiently small, a canoe dashes out from shore, dropping overboard a long screen of cocoanut leaves and encircling the circle, thus reinforcing the palisade of legs.
crsnk10{_}3079{_}Of course, the fishing is always done inside the reef in the lagoon.
crsnk10{_}3080{_}"Tres jolie," the gendarme said, after explaining by signs and gestures that thousands of fish would be caught of all sizes from minnows to sharks, and that the captured fish would boil up and upon the very sand of the beach.
crsnk10{_}3081{_}It is a most successful method of fishing, while its nature is more that of an outing festival, rather than of a prosaic, food-getting task.
crsnk10{_}3082{_}Such fishing parties take place about once a month at Bora Bora, and it is a custom that has descended from old time.
crsnk10{_}3083{_}The man who originated it is not remembered.
crsnk10{_}3084{_}They always did this thing.
crsnk10{_}3085{_}But one cannot help wondering about that forgotten savage of the long ago, into whose mind first flashed this scheme of easy fishing, of catching huge quantities of fish without hook, or net, or spear.
crsnk10{_}3086{_}One thing about him we can know:  he was a radical.
crsnk10{_}3087{_}And we can be sure that he was considered feather-brained and anarchistic by his conservative tribesmen.
crsnk10{_}3088{_}His difficulty was much greater than that of the modern inventor, who has to convince in advance only one or two capitalists.
crsnk10{_}3089{_}That early inventor had to convince his whole tribe in advance, for without the co-operation of the whole tribe the device could not be tested.
crsnk10{_}3090{_}One can well imagine the nightly pow-wow-ings in that primitive island world, when he called his comrades antiquated moss-backs, and they called him a fool, a freak, and a crank, and charged him with having come from Kansas.
crsnk10{_}3091{_}Heaven alone knows at what cost of grey hairs and expletives he must finally have succeeded in winning over a sufficient number to give his idea a trial.
crsnk10{_}3092{_}At any rate, the experiment succeeded.
crsnk10{_}3093{_}It stood the test of truth--it worked!
crsnk10{_}3094{_}And thereafter, we can be confident, there was no man to be found who did not know all along that it was going to work.
crsnk10{_}3095{_}Our good friends, Tehei and Bihaura, who were giving the fishing in our honour, had promised to come for us.
crsnk10{_}3096{_}We were down below when the call came from on deck that they were coming.
crsnk10{_}3097{_}We dashed up the companionway, to be overwhelmed by the sight of the Polynesian barge in which we were to ride.
crsnk10{_}3098{_}It was a long double canoe, the canoes lashed together by timbers with an interval of water between, and the whole decorated with flowers and golden grasses.
crsnk10{_}3099{_}A dozen flower-crowned Amazons were at the paddles, while at the stern of each canoe was a strapping steersman.
crsnk10{_}3100{_}All were garlanded with gold and crimson and orange flowers, while each wore about the hips a scarlet pareu.
crsnk10{_}3101{_}There were flowers everywhere, flowers, flowers, flowers, without out end.
crsnk10{_}3102{_}The whole thing was an orgy of colour.
crsnk10{_}3103{_}On the platform forward resting on the bows of the canoes, Tehei and Bihaura were dancing.
crsnk10{_}3104{_}All voices were raised in a wild song or greeting.
crsnk10{_}3105{_}Three times they circled the Snark before coming alongside to take Charmian and me on board.
crsnk10{_}3106{_}Then it was away for the fishing-grounds, a five-mile paddle dead to windward.
crsnk10{_}3107{_}"Everybody is jolly in Bora Bora," is the saying throughout the Society Islands, and we certainly found everybody jolly.
crsnk10{_}3108{_}Canoe songs, shark songs, and fishing songs were sung to the dipping of the paddles, all joining in on the swinging choruses.
crsnk10{_}3109{_}Once in a while the cry Mao!
crsnk10{_}3110{_}was raised, whereupon all strained like mad at the paddles.
crsnk10{_}3111{_}Mao is shark, and when the deep-sea tigers appear, the natives paddle for dear life for the shore, knowing full well the danger they run of having their frail canoes overturned and of being devoured.
crsnk10{_}3112{_}Of course, in our case there were no sharks, but the cry of mao was used to incite them to paddle with as much energy as if a shark were really after them.
crsnk10{_}3113{_}"Hoe!
crsnk10{_}3114{_}Hoe!"
crsnk10{_}3115{_}was another cry that made us foam through the water.
crsnk10{_}3116{_}On the platform Tehei and Bihaura danced, accompanied by songs and choruses or by rhythmic hand-clappings.
crsnk10{_}3117{_}At other times a musical knocking of the paddles against the sides of the canoes marked the accent.
crsnk10{_}3118{_}A young girl dropped her paddle, leaped to the platform, and danced a hula, in the midst of which, still dancing, she swayed and bent, and imprinted on our cheeks the kiss of welcome.
crsnk10{_}3119{_}Some of the songs, or himines, were religious, and they were especially beautiful, the deep basses of the men mingling with the altos and thin sopranos of the women and forming a combination of sound that irresistibly reminded one of an organ.
crsnk10{_}3120{_}In fact, "kanaka organ" is the scoffer's description of the himine.
crsnk10{_}3121{_}On the other hand, some of the chants or ballads were very barbaric, having come down from pre- Christian times.
crsnk10{_}3122{_}And so, singing, dancing, paddling, these joyous Polynesians took us to the fishing.
crsnk10{_}3123{_}The gendarme, who is the French ruler of Bora Bora, accompanied us with his family in a double canoe of his own, paddled by his prisoners; for not only is he gendarme and ruler, but he is jailer as well, and in this jolly land when anybody goes fishing, all go fishing.
crsnk10{_}3124{_}A score of single canoes, with outriggers, paddled along with us.
crsnk10{_}3125{_}Around a point a big sailing-canoe appeared, running beautifully before the wind as it bore down to greet us.
crsnk10{_}3126{_}Balancing precariously on the outrigger, three young men saluted us with a wild rolling of drums.
crsnk10{_}3127{_}The next point, half a mile farther on, brought us to the place of meeting.
crsnk10{_}3128{_}Here the launch, which had been brought along by Warren and Martin, attracted much attention.
crsnk10{_}3129{_}The Bora Borans could not see what made it go.
crsnk10{_}3130{_}The canoes were drawn upon the sand, and all hands went ashore to drink cocoanuts and sing and dance.
crsnk10{_}3131{_}Here our numbers were added to by many who arrived on foot from near-by dwellings, and a pretty sight it was to see the flower-crowned maidens, hand in hand and two by two, arriving along the sands.
crsnk10{_}3132{_}"They usually make a big catch," Allicot, a half-caste trader, told us.
crsnk10{_}3133{_}"At the finish the water is fairly alive with fish.
crsnk10{_}3134{_}It is lots of fun.
crsnk10{_}3135{_}Of course you know all the fish will be yours."
crsnk10{_}3136{_}"All?"
crsnk10{_}3137{_}I groaned, for already the Snark was loaded down with lavish presents, by the canoe-load, of fruits, vegetables, pigs, and chickens.
crsnk10{_}3138{_}"Yes, every last fish," Allicot answered.
crsnk10{_}3139{_}"You see, when the surround is completed, you, being the guest of honour, must take a harpoon and impale the first one.
crsnk10{_}3140{_}It is the custom.
crsnk10{_}3141{_}Then everybody goes in with their hands and throws the catch out on the sand.
crsnk10{_}3142{_}There will be a mountain of them.
crsnk10{_}3143{_}Then one of the chiefs will make a speech in which he presents you with the whole kit and boodle.
crsnk10{_}3144{_}But you don't have to take them all.
crsnk10{_}3145{_}You get up and make a speech, selecting what fish you want for yourself and presenting all the rest back again.
crsnk10{_}3146{_}Then everybody says you are very generous."
crsnk10{_}3147{_}"But what would be the result if I kept the whole present?"
crsnk10{_}3148{_}I asked.
crsnk10{_}3149{_}"It has never happened," was the answer.
crsnk10{_}3150{_}"It is the custom to give and give back again."
crsnk10{_}3151{_}The native minister started with a prayer for success in the fishing, and all heads were bared.
crsnk10{_}3152{_}Next, the chief fishermen told off the canoes and allotted them their places.
crsnk10{_}3153{_}Then it was into the canoes and away.
crsnk10{_}3154{_}No women, however, came along, with the exception of Bihaura and Charmian.
crsnk10{_}3155{_}In the old days even they would have been tabooed.
crsnk10{_}3156{_}The women remained behind to wade out into the water and form the palisade of legs.
crsnk10{_}3157{_}The big double canoe was left on the beech, and we went in the launch.
crsnk10{_}3158{_}Half the canoes paddled off to leeward, while we, with the other half, headed to windward a mile and a half, until the end of our line was in touch with the reef.
crsnk10{_}3159{_}The leader of the drive occupied a canoe midway in our line.
crsnk10{_}3160{_}He stood erect, a fine figure of an old man, holding a flag in his hand.
crsnk10{_}3161{_}He directed the taking of positions and the forming of the two lines by blowing on a conch.
crsnk10{_}3162{_}When all was ready, he waved his flag to the right.
crsnk10{_}3163{_}With a single splash the throwers in every canoe on that side struck the water with their stones.
crsnk10{_}3164{_}While they were hauling them back--a matter of a moment, for the stones scarcely sank beneath the surface--the flag waved to the left, and with admirable precision every stone on that side struck the water.
crsnk10{_}3165{_}So it went, back and forth, right and left; with every wave of the flag a long line of concussion smote the lagoon.
crsnk10{_}3166{_}At the same time the paddles drove the canoes forward and what was being done in our line was being done in the opposing line of canoes a mile and more away.
crsnk10{_}3167{_}On the bow of the launch, Tehei, with eyes fixed on the leader, worked his stone in unison with the others.
crsnk10{_}3168{_}Once, the stone slipped from the rope, and the same instant Tehei went overboard after it.
crsnk10{_}3169{_}I do not know whether or not that stone reached the bottom, but I do know that the next instant Tehei broke surface alongside with the stone in his hand.
crsnk10{_}3170{_}I noticed this same accident occur several times among the near-by canoes, but in each instance the thrower followed the stone and brought it back.
crsnk10{_}3171{_}The reef ends of our lines accelerated, the shore ends lagged, all under the watchful supervision of the leader, until at the reef the two lines joined, forming the circle.
crsnk10{_}3172{_}Then the contraction of the circle began, the poor frightened fish harried shoreward by the streaks of concussion that smote the water.
crsnk10{_}3173{_}In the same fashion elephants are driven through the jungle by motes of men who crouch in the long grasses or behind trees and make strange noises.
crsnk10{_}3174{_}Already the palisade of legs had been built.
crsnk10{_}3175{_}We could see the heads of the women, in a long line, dotting the placid surface of the lagoon.
crsnk10{_}3176{_}The tallest women went farthest out, thus, with the exception of those close inshore, nearly all were up to their necks in the water.
crsnk10{_}3177{_}Still the circle narrowed, till canoes were almost touching.
crsnk10{_}3178{_}There was a pause.
crsnk10{_}3179{_}A long canoe shot out from shore, following the line of the circle.
crsnk10{_}3180{_}It went as fast as paddles could drive.
crsnk10{_}3181{_}In the stern a man threw overboard the long, continuous screen of cocoanut leaves.
crsnk10{_}3182{_}The canoes were no longer needed, and overboard went the men to reinforce the palisade with their legs.
crsnk10{_}3183{_}For the screen was only a screen, and not a net, and the fish could dash through it if they tried.
crsnk10{_}3184{_}Hence the need for legs that ever agitated the screen, and for hands that splashed and throats that yelled.
crsnk10{_}3185{_}Pandemonium reigned as the trap tightened.
crsnk10{_}3186{_}But no fish broke surface or collided against the hidden legs.
crsnk10{_}3187{_}At last the chief fisherman entered the trap.
crsnk10{_}3188{_}He waded around everywhere, carefully.
crsnk10{_}3189{_}But there were no fish boiling up and out upon the sand.
crsnk10{_}3190{_}There was not a sardine, not a minnow, not a polly- wog.
crsnk10{_}3191{_}Something must have been wrong with that prayer; or else, and more likely, as one grizzled fellow put it, the wind was not in its usual quarter and the fish were elsewhere in the lagoon.
crsnk10{_}3192{_}In fact, there had been no fish to drive.
crsnk10{_}3193{_}"About once in five these drives are failures," Allicot consoled us.
crsnk10{_}3194{_}Well, it was the stone-fishing that had brought us to Bora Bora, and it was our luck to draw the one chance in five.
crsnk10{_}3195{_}Had it been a raffle, it would have been the other way about.
crsnk10{_}3196{_}This is not pessimism.
crsnk10{_}3197{_}Nor is it an indictment of the plan of the universe.
crsnk10{_}3198{_}It is merely that feeling which is familiar to most fishermen at the empty end of a hard day.
crsnk10{_}3199{_}CHAPTER XIV--THE AMATEUR NAVIGATOR    There are captains and captains, and some mighty fine captains, I know; but the run of the captains on the Snark has been remarkably otherwise.
crsnk10{_}3200{_}My experience with them has been that it is harder to take care of one captain on a small boat than of two small babies.
crsnk10{_}3201{_}Of course, this is no more than is to be expected.
crsnk10{_}3202{_}The good men have positions, and are not likely to forsake their one-thousand-to- fifteen-thousand-ton billets for the Snark with her ten tons net.
crsnk10{_}3203{_}The Snark has had to cull her navigators from the beach, and the navigator on the beach is usually a congenital inefficient--the sort of man who beats about for a fortnight trying vainly to find an ocean isle and who returns with his schooner to report the island sunk with all on board, the sort of man whose temper or thirst for strong waters works him out of billets faster than he can work into them.
crsnk10{_}3204{_}The Snark has had three captains, and by the grace of God she shall have no more.
crsnk10{_}3205{_}The first captain was so senile as to be unable to give a measurement for a boom-jaw to a carpenter.
crsnk10{_}3206{_}So utterly agedly helpless was he, that he was unable to order a sailor to throw a few buckets of salt water on the Snark's deck.
crsnk10{_}3207{_}For twelve days, at anchor, under an overhead tropic sun, the deck lay dry.
crsnk10{_}3208{_}It was a new deck.
crsnk10{_}3209{_}It cost me one hundred and thirty-five dollars to recaulk it.
crsnk10{_}3210{_}The second captain was angry.
crsnk10{_}3211{_}He was born angry.
crsnk10{_}3212{_}"Papa is always angry," was the description given him by his half-breed son.
crsnk10{_}3213{_}The third captain was so crooked that he couldn't hide behind a corkscrew.
crsnk10{_}3214{_}The truth was not in him, common honesty was not in him, and he was as far away from fair play and square-dealing as he was from his proper course when he nearly wrecked the Snark on the Ring- gold Isles.
crsnk10{_}3215{_}It was at Suva, in the Fijis, that I discharged my third and last captain and took up gain the role of amateur navigator.
crsnk10{_}3216{_}I had essayed it once before, under my first captain, who, out of San Francisco, jumped the Snark so amazingly over the chart that I really had to find out what was doing.
crsnk10{_}3217{_}It was fairly easy to find out, for we had a run of twenty-one hundred miles before us.
crsnk10{_}3218{_}I knew nothing of navigation; but, after several hours of reading up and half an hour's practice with the sextant, I was able to find the Snark's latitude by meridian observation and her longitude by the simple method known as "equal altitudes."
crsnk10{_}3219{_}This is not a correct method.
crsnk10{_}3220{_}It is not even a safe method, but my captain was attempting to navigate by it, and he was the only one on board who should have been able to tell me that it was a method to be eschewed.
crsnk10{_}3221{_}I brought the Snark to Hawaii, but the conditions favoured me.
crsnk10{_}3222{_}The sun was in northern declination and nearly overhead.
crsnk10{_}3223{_}The legitimate "chronometer-sight" method of ascertaining the longitude I had not heard of--yes, I had heard of it.
crsnk10{_}3224{_}My first captain mentioned it vaguely, but after one or two attempts at practice of it he mentioned it no more.
crsnk10{_}3225{_}I had time in the Fijis to compare my chronometer with two other chronometers.
crsnk10{_}3226{_}Two weeks previous, at Pago Pago, in Samoa, I had asked my captain to compare our chronometer with the chronometers on the American cruiser, the Annapolis.
crsnk10{_}3227{_}This he told me he had done-- of course he had done nothing of the sort; and he told me that the difference he had ascertained was only a small fraction of a second.
crsnk10{_}3228{_}He told it to me with finely simulated joy and with words of praise for my splendid time-keeper.
crsnk10{_}3229{_}I repeat it now, with words of praise for his splendid and unblushing unveracity.
crsnk10{_}3230{_}For behold, fourteen days later, in Suva, I compared the chronometer with the one on the Atua, an Australian steamer, and found that mine was thirty-one seconds fast.
crsnk10{_}3231{_}Now thirty-one seconds of time, converted into arc, equals seven and one-quarter miles.
crsnk10{_}3232{_}That is to say, if I were sailing west, in the night-time, and my position, according to my dead reckoning from my afternoon chronometer sight, was shown to be seven miles off the land, why, at that very moment I would be crashing on the reef.
crsnk10{_}3233{_}Next I compared my chronometer with Captain Wooley's.
crsnk10{_}3234{_}Captain Wooley, the harbourmaster, gives the time to Suva, firing a gun signal at twelve, noon, three times a week.
crsnk10{_}3235{_}According to his chronometer mine was fifty-nine seconds fast, which is to say, that, sailing west, I should be crashing on the reef when I thought I was fifteen miles off from it.
crsnk10{_}3236{_}I compromised by subtracting thirty-one seconds from the total of my chronometer's losing error, and sailed away for Tanna, in the New Hebrides, resolved, when nosing around the land on dark nights, to bear in mind the other seven miles I might be out according to Captain Wooley's instrument.
crsnk10{_}3237{_}Tanna lay some six hundred miles west- southwest from the Fijis, and it was my belief that while covering that distance I could quite easily knock into my head sufficient navigation to get me there.
crsnk10{_}3238{_}Well, I got there, but listen first to my troubles.
crsnk10{_}3239{_}Navigation IS easy, I shall always contend that; but when a man is taking three gasolene engines and a wife around the world and is writing hard every day to keep the engines supplied with gasolene and the wife with pearls and volcanoes, he hasn't much time left in which to study navigation.
crsnk10{_}3240{_}Also, it is bound to be easier to study said science ashore, where latitude and longitude are unchanging, in a house whose position never alters, than it is to study navigation on a boat that is rushing along day and night toward land that one is trying to find and which he is liable to find disastrously at a moment when he least expects it.
crsnk10{_}3241{_}To begin with, there are the compasses and the setting of the courses.
crsnk10{_}3242{_}We sailed from Suva on Saturday afternoon, June 6, 1908, and it took us till after dark to run the narrow, reef-ridden passage between the islands of Viti Levu and Mbengha.
crsnk10{_}3243{_}The open ocean lay before me.
crsnk10{_}3244{_}There was nothing in the way with the exception of Vatu Leile, a miserable little island that persisted in poking up through the sea some twenty miles to the west-southwest-- just where I wanted to go.
crsnk10{_}3245{_}Of course, it seemed quite simple to avoid it by steering a course that would pass it eight or ten miles to the north.
crsnk10{_}3246{_}It was a black night, and we were running before the wind.
crsnk10{_}3247{_}The man at the wheel must be told what direction to steer in order to miss Vatu Leile.
crsnk10{_}3248{_}But what direction?
crsnk10{_}3249{_}I turned me to the navigation books.
crsnk10{_}3250{_}"True Course" I lighted upon.
crsnk10{_}3251{_}The very thing!
crsnk10{_}3252{_}What I wanted was the true course.
crsnk10{_}3253{_}I read eagerly on:  "The True Course is the angle made with the meridian by a straight line on the chart drawn to connect the ship's position with the place bound to."
crsnk10{_}3254{_}Just what I wanted.
crsnk10{_}3255{_}The Snark's position was at the western entrance of the passage between Viti Levu and Mbengha.
crsnk10{_}3256{_}The immediate place she was bound to was a place on the chart ten miles north of Vatu Leile.
crsnk10{_}3257{_}I pricked that place off on the chart with my dividers, and with my parallel rulers found that west-by-south was the true course.
crsnk10{_}3258{_}I had but to give it to the man at the wheel and the Snark would win her way to the safety of the open sea.
crsnk10{_}3259{_}But alas and alack and lucky for me, I read on.
crsnk10{_}3260{_}I discovered that the compass, that trusty, everlasting friend of the mariner, was not given to pointing north.
crsnk10{_}3261{_}It varied.
crsnk10{_}3262{_}Sometimes it pointed east of north, sometimes west of north, and on occasion it even turned tail on north and pointed south.
crsnk10{_}3263{_}The variation at the particular spot on the globe occupied by the Snark was 9 degrees 40 minutes easterly.
crsnk10{_}3264{_}Well, that had to be taken in to account before I gave the steering course to the man at the wheel.
crsnk10{_}3265{_}I read:  "The Correct Magnetic Course is derived from the True Course by applying to it the variation."
crsnk10{_}3266{_}Therefore, I reasoned, if the compass points 9 degrees 40 minutes eastward of north, and I wanted to sail due north, I should have to steer 9 degrees 40 minutes westward of the north indicated by the compass and which was not north at all.
crsnk10{_}3267{_}So I added 9 degrees 40 minutes to the left of my west-by-south course, thus getting my correct Magnetic Course, and was ready once more to run to open sea.
crsnk10{_}3268{_}Again alas and alack!
crsnk10{_}3269{_}The Correct Magnetic Course was not the Compass Course.
crsnk10{_}3270{_}There was another sly little devil lying in wait to trip me up and land me smashing on the reefs of Vatu Leile.
crsnk10{_}3271{_}This little devil went by the name of Deviation.
crsnk10{_}3272{_}I read:  "The Compass Course is the course to steer, and is derived from the Correct Magnetic Course by applying to it the Deviation."
crsnk10{_}3273{_}Now Deviation is the variation in the needle caused by the distribution of iron on board of ship.
crsnk10{_}3274{_}This purely local variation I derived from the deviation card of my standard compass and then applied to the Correct Magnetic Course.
crsnk10{_}3275{_}The result was the Compass Course.
crsnk10{_}3276{_}And yet, not yet.
crsnk10{_}3277{_}My standard compass was amidships on the companionway.
crsnk10{_}3278{_}My steering compass was aft, in the cockpit, near the wheel.
crsnk10{_}3279{_}When the steering compass pointed west-by-south three- quarters-south (the steering course), the standard compass pointed west-one-half-north, which was certainly not the steering course.
crsnk10{_}3280{_}I kept the Snark up till she was heading west-by-south-three-quarters- south on the standard compass, which gave, on the steering compass, south-west-by-west.
crsnk10{_}3281{_}The foregoing operations constitute the simple little matter of setting a course.
crsnk10{_}3282{_}And the worst of it is that one must perform every step correctly or else he will hear "Breakers ahead!"
crsnk10{_}3283{_}some pleasant night, a nice sea-bath, and be given the delightful diversion of fighting his way to the shore through a horde of man- eating sharks.
crsnk10{_}3284{_}Just as the compass is tricky and strives to fool the mariner by pointing in all directions except north, so does that guide post of the sky, the sun, persist in not being where it ought to be at a given time.
crsnk10{_}3285{_}This carelessness of the sun is the cause of more trouble--at least it caused trouble for me.
crsnk10{_}3286{_}To find out where one is on the earth's surface, he must know, at precisely the same time, where the sun is in the heavens.
crsnk10{_}3287{_}That is to say, the sun, which is the timekeeper for men, doesn't run on time.
crsnk10{_}3288{_}When I discovered this, I fell into deep gloom and all the Cosmos was filled with doubt.
crsnk10{_}3289{_}Immutable laws, such as gravitation and the conservation of energy, became wobbly, and I was prepared to witness their violation at any moment and to remain unastonished.
crsnk10{_}3290{_}For see, if the compass lied and the sun did not keep its engagements, why should not objects lose their mutual attraction and why should not a few bushel baskets of force be annihilated?
crsnk10{_}3291{_}Even perpetual motion became possible, and I was in a frame of mind prone to purchase Keeley- Motor stock from the first enterprising agent that landed on the Snark's deck.
crsnk10{_}3292{_}And when I discovered that the earth really rotated on its axis 366 times a year, while there were only 365 sunrises and sunsets, I was ready to doubt my own identity.
crsnk10{_}3293{_}This is the way of the sun.
crsnk10{_}3294{_}It is so irregular that it is impossible for man to devise a clock that will keep the sun's time.
crsnk10{_}3295{_}The sun accelerates and retards as no clock could be made to accelerate and retard.
crsnk10{_}3296{_}The sun is sometimes ahead of its schedule; at other times it is lagging behind; and at still other times it is breaking the speed limit in order to overtake itself, or, rather, to catch up with where it ought to be in the sky.
crsnk10{_}3297{_}In this last case it does not slow down quick enough, and, as a result, goes dashing ahead of where it ought to be.
crsnk10{_}3298{_}In fact, only four days in a year do the sun and the place where the sun ought to be happen to coincide.
crsnk10{_}3299{_}The remaining 361 days the sun is pothering around all over the shop.
crsnk10{_}3300{_}Man, being more perfect than the sun, makes a clock that keeps regular time.
crsnk10{_}3301{_}Also, he calculates how far the sun is ahead of its schedule or behind.
crsnk10{_}3302{_}The difference between the sun's position and the position where the sun ought to be if it were a decent, self-respecting sun, man calls the Equation of Time.
crsnk10{_}3303{_}Thus, the navigator endeavouring to find his ship's position on the sea, looks in his chronometer to see where precisely the sun ought to be according to the Greenwich custodian of the sun.
crsnk10{_}3304{_}Then to that location he applies the Equation of Time and finds out where the sun ought to be and isn't.
crsnk10{_}3305{_}This latter location, along with several other locations, enables him to find out what the man from Kansas demanded to know some years ago.
crsnk10{_}3306{_}The Snark sailed from Fiji on Saturday, June 6, and the next day, Sunday, on the wide ocean, out of sight of land, I proceeded to endeavour to find out my position by a chronometer sight for longitude and by a meridian observation for latitude.
crsnk10{_}3307{_}The chronometer sight was taken in the morning when the sun was some 21 degrees above the horizon.
crsnk10{_}3308{_}I looked in the Nautical Almanac and found that on that very day, June 7, the sun was behind time 1 minute and 26 seconds, and that it was catching up at a rate of 14.67 seconds per hour.
crsnk10{_}3309{_}The chronometer said that at the precise moment of taking the sun's altitude it was twenty-five minutes after eight o'clock at Greenwich.
crsnk10{_}3310{_}From this date it would seem a schoolboy's task to correct the Equation of Time.
crsnk10{_}3311{_}Unfortunately, I was not a schoolboy.
crsnk10{_}3312{_}Obviously, at the middle of the day, at Greenwich, the sun was 1 minute and 26 seconds behind time.
crsnk10{_}3313{_}Equally obviously, if it were eleven o'clock in the morning, the sun would be 1 minute and 26 seconds behind time plus 14.67 seconds.
crsnk10{_}3314{_}If it were ten o'clock in the morning, twice 14.67 seconds would have to be added.
crsnk10{_}3315{_}And if it were 8:  25 in the morning, then 3.5 times 14.67 seconds would have to be added.
crsnk10{_}3316{_}Quite clearly, then, if, instead of being 8:25 A.M., it were 8:25 P.M., then 8.5 times 14.67 seconds would have to be, not added, but SUBTRACTED; for, if, at noon, the sun were 1 minute and 26 seconds behind time, and if it were catching up with where it ought to be at the rate of 14.67 seconds per hour, then at 8.25 P.M. it would be much nearer where it ought to be than it had been at noon.
crsnk10{_}3317{_}So far, so good.
crsnk10{_}3318{_}But was that 8:25 of the chronometer A.M., or P.M.?
crsnk10{_}3319{_}I looked at the Snark's clock.
crsnk10{_}3320{_}It marked 8:9, and it was certainly A.M. for I had just finished breakfast.
crsnk10{_}3321{_}Therefore, if it was eight in the morning on board the Snark, the eight o'clock of the chronometer (which was the time of the day at Greenwich) must be a different eight o'clock from the Snark's eight o'clock.
crsnk10{_}3322{_}But what eight o'clock was it?
crsnk10{_}3323{_}It can't be the eight o'clock of this morning, I reasoned; therefore, it must be either eight o'clock this evening or eight o'clock last night.
crsnk10{_}3324{_}It was at this juncture that I fell into the bottomless pit of intellectual chaos.
crsnk10{_}3325{_}We are in east longitude, I reasoned, therefore we are ahead of Greenwich.
crsnk10{_}3326{_}If we are behind Greenwich, then to-day is yesterday; if we are ahead of Greenwich, then yesterday is to- day, but if yesterday is to-day, what under the sun is to-day!--to- morrow?
crsnk10{_}3327{_}Absurd!
crsnk10{_}3328{_}Yet it must be correct.
crsnk10{_}3329{_}When I took the sun this morning at 8:25, the sun's custodians at Greenwich were just arising from dinner last night.
crsnk10{_}3330{_}"Then correct the Equation of Time for yesterday," says my logical mind.
crsnk10{_}3331{_}"But to-day is to-day," my literal mind insists.
crsnk10{_}3332{_}"I must correct the sun for to-day and not for yesterday."
crsnk10{_}3333{_}"Yet to-day is yesterday," urges my logical mind.
crsnk10{_}3334{_}"That's all very well," my literal mind continues, "If I were in Greenwich I might be in yesterday.
crsnk10{_}3335{_}Strange things happen in Greenwich.
crsnk10{_}3336{_}But I know as sure as I am living that I am here, now, in to-day, June 7, and that I took the sun here, now, to-day, June 7.
crsnk10{_}3337{_}Therefore, I must correct the sun here, now, to-day, June 7."
crsnk10{_}3338{_}"Bosh!"
crsnk10{_}3339{_}snaps my logical mind.
crsnk10{_}3340{_}"Lecky says--"  "Never mind what Lecky says," interrupts my literal mind.
crsnk10{_}3341{_}"Let me tell you what the Nautical Almanac says.
crsnk10{_}3342{_}The Nautical Almanac says that to-day, June 7, the sun was 1 minute and 26 seconds behind time and catching up at the rate of 14.67 seconds per hour.
crsnk10{_}3343{_}It says that yesterday, June 6, the sun was 1 minute and 36 seconds behind time and catching up at the rate of 15.66 seconds per hour.
crsnk10{_}3344{_}You see, it is preposterous to think of correcting to-day's sun by yesterday's time-table."
crsnk10{_}3345{_}"Fool!"
crsnk10{_}3346{_}"Idiot!"
crsnk10{_}3347{_}Back and forth they wrangle until my head is whirling around and I am ready to believe that I am in the day after the last week before next.
crsnk10{_}3348{_}I remembered a parting caution of the Suva harbour-master:  "IN EAST LONGITUDE TAKE FROM THE NAUTICAL ALMANAC THE ELEMENTS FOR THE PRECEDING DAY."
crsnk10{_}3349{_}Then a new thought came to me.
crsnk10{_}3350{_}I corrected the Equation of Time for Sunday and for Saturday, making two separate operations of it, and lo, when the results were compared, there was a difference only of four-tenths of a second.
crsnk10{_}3351{_}I was a changed man.
crsnk10{_}3352{_}I had found my way out of the crypt.
crsnk10{_}3353{_}The Snark was scarcely big enough to hold me and my experience.
crsnk10{_}3354{_}Four-tenths of a second would make a difference of only one-tenth of a mile--a cable-length!
crsnk10{_}3355{_}All went merrily for ten minutes, when I chanced upon the following rhyme for navigators:   "Greenwich time least Longitude east; Greenwich best, Longitude west."
crsnk10{_}3356{_}Heavens!
crsnk10{_}3357{_}The Snark's time was not as good as Greenwich time.
crsnk10{_}3358{_}When it was 8 25 at Greenwich, on board the Snark it was only 8:9.
crsnk10{_}3359{_}"Greenwich time best, longitude west."
crsnk10{_}3360{_}There I was.
crsnk10{_}3361{_}In west longitude beyond a doubt.
crsnk10{_}3362{_}"Silly!"
crsnk10{_}3363{_}cries my literal mind.
crsnk10{_}3364{_}"You are 8:9 A.M. and Greenwich is 8:25 P.M."  "Very well," answers my logical mind.
crsnk10{_}3365{_}"To be correct, 8.25 P.M. is really twenty hours and twenty-five minutes, and that is certainly better than eight hours and nine minutes.
crsnk10{_}3366{_}No, there is no discussion; you are in west longitude."
crsnk10{_}3367{_}Then my literal mind triumphs.
crsnk10{_}3368{_}"We sailed from Suva, in the Fijis, didn't we?"
crsnk10{_}3369{_}it demands, and logical mind agrees.
crsnk10{_}3370{_}"And Suva is in east longitude?"
crsnk10{_}3371{_}Again logical mind agrees.
crsnk10{_}3372{_}"And we sailed west (which would take us deeper into east longitude), didn't we?
crsnk10{_}3373{_}Therefore, and you can't escape it, we are in east longitude."
crsnk10{_}3374{_}"Greenwich time best, longitude west," chants my logical mind; "and you must grant that twenty hours and twenty-five minutes is better than eight hours and nine minutes."
crsnk10{_}3375{_}"All right," I break in upon the squabble; "we'll work up the sight and then we'll see."
crsnk10{_}3376{_}And work it up I did, only to find that my longitude was 184 degrees west.
crsnk10{_}3377{_}"I told you so," snorts my logical mind.
crsnk10{_}3378{_}I am dumbfounded.
crsnk10{_}3379{_}So is my literal mind, for several minutes.
crsnk10{_}3380{_}Then it enounces:  "But there is no 184 degrees west longitude, nor east longitude, nor any other longitude.
crsnk10{_}3381{_}The largest meridian is 180 degrees as you ought to know very well."
crsnk10{_}3382{_}Having got this far, literal mind collapses from the brain strain, logical mind is dumb flabbergasted; and as for me, I get a bleak and wintry look in my eyes and go around wondering whether I am sailing toward the China coast or the Gulf of Darien.
crsnk10{_}3383{_}Then a thin small voice, which I do not recognize, coming from nowhere in particular in my consciousness, says:  "The total number of degrees is 360.
crsnk10{_}3384{_}Subtract the 184 degrees west longitude from 360 degrees, and you will get 176 degrees east longitude."
crsnk10{_}3385{_}"That is sheer speculation," objects literal mind; and logical mind remonstrates.
crsnk10{_}3386{_}"There is no rule for it."
crsnk10{_}3387{_}"Darn the rules!"
crsnk10{_}3388{_}I exclaim.
crsnk10{_}3389{_}"Ain't I here?"
crsnk10{_}3390{_}"The thing is self-evident," I continue.
crsnk10{_}3391{_}"184 degrees west longitude means a lapping over in east longitude of four degrees.
crsnk10{_}3392{_}Besides I have been in east longitude all the time.
crsnk10{_}3393{_}I sailed from Fiji, and Fiji is in east longitude.
crsnk10{_}3394{_}Now I shall chart my position and prove it by dead reckoning."
crsnk10{_}3395{_}But other troubles and doubts awaited me.
crsnk10{_}3396{_}Here is a sample of one.
crsnk10{_}3397{_}In south latitude, when the sun is in northern declination, chronometer sights may be taken early in the morning.
crsnk10{_}3398{_}I took mine at eight o'clock.
crsnk10{_}3399{_}Now, one of the necessary elements in working up such a sight is latitude.
crsnk10{_}3400{_}But one gets latitude at twelve o'clock, noon, by a meridian observation.
crsnk10{_}3401{_}It is clear that in order to work up my eight o'clock chronometer sight I must have my eight o'clock latitude.
crsnk10{_}3402{_}Of course, if the Snark were sailing due west at six knots per hour, for the intervening four hours her latitude would not change.
crsnk10{_}3403{_}But if she were sailing due south, her latitude would change to the tune of twenty-four miles.
crsnk10{_}3404{_}In which case a simple addition or subtraction would convert the twelve o'clock latitude into eight o'clock latitude.
crsnk10{_}3405{_}But suppose the Snark were sailing southwest.
crsnk10{_}3406{_}Then the traverse tables must be consulted.
crsnk10{_}3407{_}This is the illustration.
crsnk10{_}3408{_}At eight A.M.
crsnk10{_}3409{_}I took my chronometer sight.
crsnk10{_}3410{_}At the same moment the distance recorded on the log was noted.
crsnk10{_}3411{_}At twelve M., when the sight for latitude was taken.
crsnk10{_}3412{_}I again noted the log, which showed me that since eight o'clock the Snark had run 24 miles.
crsnk10{_}3413{_}Her true course had been west 0.75 south.
crsnk10{_}3414{_}I entered Table I, in the distance column, on the page for 0.75 point courses, and stopped at 24, the number of miles run.
crsnk10{_}3415{_}Opposite, in the next two columns, I found that the Snark had made 3.5 miles of southing or latitude, and that she had made 23.7 miles of westing.
crsnk10{_}3416{_}To find my eight o'clock' latitude was easy.
crsnk10{_}3417{_}I had but to subtract 3.5 miles from my noon latitude.
crsnk10{_}3418{_}All the elements being present, I worked up my longitude.
crsnk10{_}3419{_}But this was my eight o'clock longitude.
crsnk10{_}3420{_}Since then, and up till noon, I had made 23.7 miles of westing.
crsnk10{_}3421{_}What was my noon longitude?
crsnk10{_}3422{_}I followed the rule, turning to Traverse Table No.
crsnk10{_}3423{_}II.
crsnk10{_}3424{_}Entering the table, according to rule, and going through every detail, according to rule, I found the difference of longitude for the four hours to be 25 miles.
crsnk10{_}3425{_}I was aghast.
crsnk10{_}3426{_}I entered the table again, according to rule; I entered the table half a dozen times, according to rule, and every time found that my difference of longitude was 25 miles.
crsnk10{_}3427{_}I leave it to you, gentle reader.
crsnk10{_}3428{_}Suppose you had sailed 24 miles and that you had covered 3.5 miles of latitude, then how could you have covered 25 miles of longitude?
crsnk10{_}3429{_}Even if you had sailed due west 24 miles, and not changed your latitude, how could you have changed your longitude 25 miles?
crsnk10{_}3430{_}In the name of human reason, how could you cover one mile more of longitude than the total number of miles you had sailed?
crsnk10{_}3431{_}It was a reputable traverse table, being none other than Bowditch's.
crsnk10{_}3432{_}The rule was simple (as navigators' rules go); I had made no error.
crsnk10{_}3433{_}I spent an hour over it, and at the end still faced the glaring impossibility of having sailed 24 miles, in the course of which I changed my latitude 3.5 miles and my longitude 25 miles.
crsnk10{_}3434{_}The worst of it was that there was nobody to help me out.
crsnk10{_}3435{_}Neither Charmian nor Martin knew as much as I knew about navigation.
crsnk10{_}3436{_}And all the time the Snark was rushing madly along toward Tanna, in the New Hebrides.
crsnk10{_}3437{_}Something had to be done.
crsnk10{_}3438{_}How it came to me I know not--call it an inspiration if you will; but the thought arose in me:  if southing is latitude, why isn't westing longitude?
crsnk10{_}3439{_}Why should I have to change westing into longitude?
crsnk10{_}3440{_}And then the whole beautiful situation dawned upon me.
crsnk10{_}3441{_}The meridians of longitude are 60 miles (nautical) apart at the equator.
crsnk10{_}3442{_}At the poles they run together.
crsnk10{_}3443{_}Thus, if I should travel up the 180 degrees meridian of longitude until I reached the North Pole, and if the astronomer at Greenwich travelled up the 0 meridian of longitude to the North Pole, then, at the North Pole, we could shake hands with each other, though before we started for the North Pole we had been some thousands of miles apart.
crsnk10{_}3444{_}Again:  if a degree of longitude was 60 miles wide at the equator, and if the same degree, at the point of the Pole, had no width, then somewhere between the Pole and the equator that degree would be half a mile wide, and at other places a mile wide, two miles wide, ten miles wide, thirty miles wide, ay, and sixty miles wide.
crsnk10{_}3445{_}All was plain again.
crsnk10{_}3446{_}The Snark was in 19 degrees south latitude.
crsnk10{_}3447{_}The world wasn't as big around there as at the equator.
crsnk10{_}3448{_}Therefore, every mile of westing at 19 degrees south was more than a minute of longitude; for sixty miles were sixty miles, but sixty minutes are sixty miles only at the equator.
crsnk10{_}3449{_}George Francis Train broke Jules Verne's record of around the world.
crsnk10{_}3450{_}But any man that wants can break George Francis Train's record.
crsnk10{_}3451{_}Such a man would need only to go, in a fast steamer, to the latitude of Cape Horn, and sail due east all the way around.
crsnk10{_}3452{_}The world is very small in that latitude, and there is no land in the way to turn him out of his course.
crsnk10{_}3453{_}If his steamer maintained sixteen knots, he would circumnavigate the globe in just about forty days.
crsnk10{_}3454{_}But there are compensations.
crsnk10{_}3455{_}On Wednesday evening, June 10, I brought up my noon position by dead reckoning to eight P.M.  Then I projected the Snark's course and saw that she would strike Futuna, one of the easternmost of the New Hebrides, a volcanic cone two thousand feet high that rose out of the deep ocean.
crsnk10{_}3456{_}I altered the course so that the Snark would pass ten miles to the northward.
crsnk10{_}3457{_}Then I spoke to Wada, the cook, who had the wheel every morning from four to six.
crsnk10{_}3458{_}"Wada San, to-morrow morning, your watch, you look sharp on weather- bow you see land."
crsnk10{_}3459{_}And then I went to bed.
crsnk10{_}3460{_}The die was cast.
crsnk10{_}3461{_}I had staked my reputation as a navigator.
crsnk10{_}3462{_}Suppose, just suppose, that at daybreak there was no land.
crsnk10{_}3463{_}Then, where would my navigation be?
crsnk10{_}3464{_}And where would we be?
crsnk10{_}3465{_}And how would we ever find ourselves?
crsnk10{_}3466{_}or find any land?
crsnk10{_}3467{_}I caught ghastly visions of the Snark sailing for months through ocean solitudes and seeking vainly for land while we consumed our provisions and sat down with haggard faces to stare cannibalism in the face.
crsnk10{_}3468{_}I confess my sleep was not   " .
crsnk10{_}3469{_}.
crsnk10{_}3470{_}.
crsnk10{_}3471{_}like a summer sky That held the music of a lark."
crsnk10{_}3472{_}Rather did "I waken to the voiceless dark," and listen to the creaking of the bulkheads and the rippling of the sea alongside as the Snark logged steadily her six knots an hour.
crsnk10{_}3473{_}I went over my calculations again and again, striving to find some mistake, until my brain was in such fever that it discovered dozens of mistakes.
crsnk10{_}3474{_}Suppose, instead of being sixty miles off Futuna, that my navigation was all wrong and that I was only six miles off?
crsnk10{_}3475{_}In which case my course would be wrong, too, and for all I knew the Snark might be running straight at Futuna.
crsnk10{_}3476{_}For all I knew the Snark might strike Futuna the next moment.
crsnk10{_}3477{_}I almost sprang from the bunk at that thought; and, though I restrained myself, I know that I lay for a moment, nervous and tense, waiting for the shock.
crsnk10{_}3478{_}My sleep was broken by miserable nightmares.
crsnk10{_}3479{_}Earthquake seemed the favourite affliction, though there was one man, with a bill, who persisted in dunning me throughout the night.
crsnk10{_}3480{_}Also, he wanted to fight; and Charmian continually persuaded me to let him alone.
crsnk10{_}3481{_}Finally, however, the man with the everlasting dun ventured into a dream from which Charmian was absent.
crsnk10{_}3482{_}It was my opportunity, and we went at it, gloriously, all over the sidewalk and street, until he cried enough.
crsnk10{_}3483{_}Then I said, "Now how about that bill?"
crsnk10{_}3484{_}Having conquered, I was willing to pay.
crsnk10{_}3485{_}But the man looked at me and groaned.
crsnk10{_}3486{_}"It was all a mistake," he said; "the bill is for the house next door."
crsnk10{_}3487{_}That settled him, for he worried my dreams no more; and it settled me, too, for I woke up chuckling at the episode.
crsnk10{_}3488{_}It was three in the morning.
crsnk10{_}3489{_}I went up on deck.
crsnk10{_}3490{_}Henry, the Rapa islander, was steering.
crsnk10{_}3491{_}I looked at the log.
crsnk10{_}3492{_}It recorded forty-two miles.
crsnk10{_}3493{_}The Snark had not abated her six-knot gait, and she had not struck Futuna yet.
crsnk10{_}3494{_}At half-past five I was again on deck.
crsnk10{_}3495{_}Wada, at the wheel, had seen no land.
crsnk10{_}3496{_}I sat on the cockpit rail, a prey to morbid doubt for a quarter of an hour.
crsnk10{_}3497{_}Then I saw land, a small, high piece of land, just where it ought to be, rising from the water on the weather-bow.
crsnk10{_}3498{_}At six o'clock I could clearly make it out to be the beautiful volcanic cone of Futuna.
crsnk10{_}3499{_}At eight o'clock, when it was abreast, I took its distance by the sextant and found it to be 9.3 miles away.
crsnk10{_}3500{_}And I had elected to pass it 10 miles away!
crsnk10{_}3501{_}Then, to the south, Aneiteum rose out of the sea, to the north, Aniwa, and, dead ahead, Tanna.
crsnk10{_}3502{_}There was no mistaking Tanna, for the smoke of its volcano was towering high in the sky.
crsnk10{_}3503{_}It was forty miles away, and by afternoon, as we drew close, never ceasing to log our six knots, we saw that it was a mountainous, hazy land, with no apparent openings in its coast-line.
crsnk10{_}3504{_}I was looking for Port Resolution, though I was quite prepared to find that as an anchorage, it had been destroyed.
crsnk10{_}3505{_}Volcanic earthquakes had lifted its bottom during the last forty years, so that where once the largest ships rode at anchor there was now, by last reports, scarcely space and depth sufficient for the Snark.
crsnk10{_}3506{_}And why should not another convulsion, since the last report, have closed the harbour completely?
crsnk10{_}3507{_}I ran in close to the unbroken coast, fringed with rocks awash upon which the crashing trade-wind sea burst white and high.
crsnk10{_}3508{_}I searched with my glasses for miles, but could see no entrance.
crsnk10{_}3509{_}I took a compass bearing of Futuna, another of Aniwa, and laid them off on the chart.
crsnk10{_}3510{_}Where the two bearings crossed was bound to be the position of the Snark.
crsnk10{_}3511{_}Then, with my parallel rulers, I laid down a course from the Snark's position to Port Resolution.
crsnk10{_}3512{_}Having corrected this course for variation and deviation, I went on deck, and lo, the course directed me towards that unbroken coast-line of bursting seas.
crsnk10{_}3513{_}To my Rapa islander's great concern, I held on till the rocks awash were an eighth of a mile away.
crsnk10{_}3514{_}"No harbour this place," he announced, shaking his head ominously.
crsnk10{_}3515{_}But I altered the course and ran along parallel with the coast.
crsnk10{_}3516{_}Charmian was at the wheel.
crsnk10{_}3517{_}Martin was at the engine, ready to throw on the propeller.
crsnk10{_}3518{_}A narrow silt of an opening showed up suddenly.
crsnk10{_}3519{_}Through the glasses I could see the seas breaking clear across.
crsnk10{_}3520{_}Henry, the Rapa man, looked with troubled eyes; so did Tehei, the Tahaa man.
crsnk10{_}3521{_}"No passage, there," said Henry.
crsnk10{_}3522{_}"We go there, we finish quick, sure."
crsnk10{_}3523{_}I confess I thought so, too; but I ran on abreast, watching to see if the line of breakers from one side the entrance did not overlap the line from the other side.
crsnk10{_}3524{_}Sure enough, it did.
crsnk10{_}3525{_}A narrow place where the sea ran smooth appeared.
crsnk10{_}3526{_}Charmian put down the wheel and steadied for the entrance.
crsnk10{_}3527{_}Martin threw on the engine, while all hands and the cook sprang to take in sail.
crsnk10{_}3528{_}A trader's house showed up in the bight of the bay.
crsnk10{_}3529{_}A geyser, on the shore, a hundred yards away; spouted a column of steam.
crsnk10{_}3530{_}To port, as we rounded a tiny point, the mission station appeared.
crsnk10{_}3531{_}"Three fathoms," cried Wada at the lead-line.
crsnk10{_}3532{_}"Three fathoms," "two fathoms," came in quick succession.
crsnk10{_}3533{_}Charmian put the wheel down, Martin stopped the engine, and the Snark rounded to and the anchor rumbled down in three fathoms.
